Apparent Motion: The Moon and Cloud Illusion
Razi explains the optical illusion of apparent motion due to relative motion with a geometric example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how relative motion between two objects can create the illusion that an object is moving in the opposite direction of its actual motion.

Core Idea
Our senses can be deceived by lack of reference points in the sky.

Razi presents a fascinating example of visual illusion that arises from relative motion. When we observe the moon near a moving cloud, we often perceive the moon as moving toward the cloud, even though it is actually moving in the opposite direction. This passage explains the geometric reason behind this deception, highlighting how the alignment of our visual ray with the moving cloud creates a false impression. Understanding this helps refine our grasp of how sensory perception can mislead us about the true nature of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the phenomenon description
Read the first part of the passage (Arabic and translation) that states the phenomenon: a traveler sees a star moving toward him, and the moon appears to move toward the cloud.
Notice that Razi sets up the problem with a simple observation.
2
Identify the geometric setup
Look at the labeled points A, B, Z, H in the commentary. The traveler moves from A to B, the moon is at J (like a point), and the cloud is at DH. Understand the initial and final positions of the visual ray.
It may help to sketch the positions on paper.
3
Trace the illusion for the moving observer
Follow Razi's explanation: when the traveler is at A, his ray aligns with Z and H; at B, the ray shifts. The changing alignment makes him imagine the moon moved from Z to H.
Razi emphasizes that the traveler does not perceive the moon's actual motion because its motion is in the opposite direction.
4
Analyze the stationary observer case
Now consider an observer at rest. The cloud moves, and the moon appears to move because the cloud's position relative to the moon changes. The lack of distinct features in the sky and cloud conceals the cloud's motion.
This is the key to the illusion: without reference points, we attribute the change to the moon.
5
Reflect on broader implications
Think about how this example illustrates the limitations of sensory knowledge. In kalam, such examples show why empirical observation alone cannot be the basis for certainty.
This connects to the rational foundation of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد نرى المتحرك إلى جهة متحركا إلى ضد تلك الجهة فإن المتحرك إلى جهة يرى الكوكب متحركا إليها إذا شاهد فيما تحته وإن كان الكوكب متحركا إلى خلاف تلك الجهة، وقد نرى القمر كالسائر إلى الغيم وإن كان سائرا إلى خلاف تلك الجهة إذا كان الغيم سائرا إليه
(2) أقول: ليسكن السائر إلى جهة ينتقل من ا إلى ب والقمر بالقياس إليه مثلج والغيم المتوسط بينهما الذي لا يحجب القمر لوقته مثل ده فإذا كان السائر عند ا كان شعاعه الممتد الذي به يرى القمر كخط أزح وإذا انتقل إلى ب صار شعاعه كخط بحد فيتخيل أن القمر تحرك من ز إلى ح في جهة حركته إذ كان أولا محاذيا لنقطة ز ثم منتقلا منها إلى ح وأما القمر المتحرك إلى خلاف تلك الجهة فلا يحس بحركته السائر، وأيضا ليكن الناظر ساكنا عند نقطة ورأى القمر وهو ج محاذيا لنقطة ز من الغيم ثم تحرك الغيم في جهة ووصلت نقطة ح إلى حيث كان في الأول نقطة ز رأى القمر محاذية لنقطة ز إلى محاذاة نقطة ح فيتخيل أن القمر يتحرك من ز إلى حركة الغيم ولا يحس بحركة الغيم لأن انتقاله في المحاذاة بالقياس إلى السماء لا يتغير في حسه لتشابه أجزاء السماء وأجزاء الغيم في الحس، وإذا كان الغيم مثل ح فقط والناظر عند أ رأى القمر بعيدا من طرف الغيم بقدر زح ثم تحرك الغيم إلى أن وصل مبدأه وهو نقطة ح إلى الموضع الذي كان فيه ز رأى القمر ح فيتخيل أن القمر متحرك من ز إلى ح فسار إلى جهة الغيم وهو خلاف جهة حركة الغيم
And we may see the moving in one direction as moving in the opposite direction: for the traveler in one direction sees the star moving toward him when he looks at what is beneath him, even if the star is moving opposite to that direction. And we may see the moon as moving toward the cloud even though it is moving opposite to that direction, when the cloud is moving toward it. (2) I say: Let the traveler in one direction be moving from A to B, and the moon relative to him be like J, and the intervening cloud which does not obstruct the moon at its time be like DH. So when the traveler is at A, his extending ray by which he sees the moon is like line AZH. When he moves to B, his ray becomes like line BHD. So he imagines that the moon moved from Z to H in the direction of his motion, since it was first aligned with point Z then moved from it to H. As for the moon moving opposite to that direction, the traveler does not perceive its motion. Also, let the observer be stationary at a point and he sees the moon (J) aligned with point Z from the cloud. Then the cloud moves in one direction and point H reaches where point Z was initially. He sees the moon aligned with point Z then aligned with point H. So he imagines that the moon moves from Z in the direction of the cloud's motion, and he does not perceive the cloud's motion because its change in alignment relative to the sky does not change in his sense due to the similarity of parts of the sky and parts of the cloud in sensation. And if the cloud is like H only, and the observer at A sees the moon far from the cloud's edge by Z H, then the cloud moves until its beginning, point H, reaches the position where Z was, then he sees the moon at H, so he imagines that the moon moved from Z to H, thus it moved in the direction of the cloud, which is opposite to the actual direction of the cloud's motion.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage addresses the illusion where an object moving in one direction appears to move in the opposite direction relative to another moving object. Razi gives two examples: a traveler looking at a star from a moving vehicle may see the star moving opposite to his own motion; and the moon may appear to move toward the cloud when the cloud is actually moving toward the moon. In the detailed footnote, he provides a geometric explanation. Using labeled points (A, B, Z, H, etc.), he explains that the changing alignment of the visual ray relative to moving objects causes the soul to misinterpret motion. The observer does not detect the cloud's motion because the sky and cloud lack distinct features to register the change. Thus, the apparent motion is attributed to the moon. This illustrates how relative motion and lack of reference points can deceive the senses.

Correction: The moon does not change direction; the cloud moves toward the moon, and the observer misattributes the changing alignment to the moon due to lack of visible reference.

B) The cloud moves toward the moon, and the observer does not perceive the cloud's motion due to lack of reference.
C) The observer's eye is at fault.
D) The moon is stationary relative to the Earth.

Key Takeaway
Perception of motion depends on reference points; without them, we can mistake one moving object for another due to relative motion.

Seeing Straight as Bent: Trees in Water
Razi explains the visual illusion of straight trees appearing bent due to reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reflection on water causes straight objects to appear bent, and why this illusion occurs according to Razi's geometric explanation.

Core Idea
Reflection bends light, and our eyes see that bend as a bent object.

Razi presents a familiar optical illusion: a straight tree on a riverbank appears bent when viewed in the water. He then begins a geometric analysis involving visual rays, reflection angles, and the positions of the observer and the tree. Although the text is incomplete, we can grasp the core idea that equal angles of incidence and reflection create the illusion of bending.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the literal translation. Notice that Razi uses a diagram-based geometric reasoning.
The text cuts off, but the main claim is clear.
2
Identify the key elements in the geometry
Identify the observer (A), the water surface (B D), and the tree (Y D). Understand that Razi sets up points to show how the visual ray reflects from the water to different parts of the tree.
The angles of incidence and reflection are equal, which is crucial.
3
Understand the illusion mechanism
The ray reflects to the top of the tree from a nearer point and to the base from a farther point, making the tree appear bent. Ask yourself why equal angles cause this.
4
Connect to the common misunderstanding
The passage corrects the misconception that the tree itself is bent. Reflect on how this illusion relates to broader themes of sensory reliability.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And we may see the straight as bent, like the trees on the banks of rivers. (3) I say: When the visual ray is reflected from the water surface to the trees such that the two angles of incidence and reflection are equal, the ray is reflected to the top of the tree from a point nearer to the observer and to its bottom from a point farther away, until the base of the tree connects with the base of its reflection. So let the observer be A, the water surface B D, and the tree standing on that surface Y D, and let the penetrating ray be reflected from A to a point... from it to the top of the tree, which is point F, such that the two angles AH B and Y D are equal. I say: It is impossible for a ray to be reflected from A to a lower part of the tree than the top, like a point...

Commentary
This final passage addresses the phenomenon of straight objects appearing bent when viewed in water, such as trees on riverbanks. Razi states that we see the straight as bent. In the footnote, he begins a geometric explanation of reflection. He says that when a visual ray reflects from the water surface to the tree, the angles of incidence and reflection are equal. The ray hits the top of the tree from a closer point to the observer, and the base from a farther point, creating an apparent bend. He then sets up a diagram with points A (observer), B D (water surface), Y D (tree), and attempts to show why the ray cannot reflect to a lower part except in a certain way. The text cuts off due to OCR incompleteness. This demonstrates another sensory illusion caused by the physics of light reflection.

The Paradoxes of Apparent Motion
Razi presents two examples of sensory illusion in motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the senses can be deceived in judging motion, as illustrated by the ship and moon examples.

Core Idea
The senses can make a moving ship appear stationary and a stationary moon appear moving.

This lesson introduces the phenomena of sensory illusion in motion perception. We will look at how a ship passenger feels stationary while the shore appears to move, and how the moon can appear to move towards clouds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the examples come through.
The passage begins with a concise statement of two illusions.
2
Identify the two illusions
Find the illusion of the ship passenger and the illusion of the moon. Notice how each is described.
The first illusion involves a moving ship appearing stationary, and the second involves the moon appearing to move towards clouds.
3
Reflect on the paradox
Ask yourself: Why are these considered illusions? What does this say about the reliability of the senses?
These examples challenge the idea that sensory perception is always accurate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متحركا والسفينة المتحركة ساكنة (۱) وقد نرى المتحرك إلى جهة متحركا إلى ضد تلك الجهة فإن المتحرك إلى جهة يرى الكوكب متحركا إليها إذا شاهد فيما تحته وإن كان الكوكب متحركا إلى خلاف تلك الجهة، وقد نرى القمر كالسائر إلى الغيم وإن كان سائرا إلى خلاف تلك الجهة إذا كان الغيم سائرا إليه (۲). أقول: الحركة ليست بمرئية، والبصر إذا أدرك الشيء في موضع محازيا لشيء ما بعد أن أدركه في موضع آخر محاذيا لغير ذلك الشيء حكمت النفس عند مجموع الإدراكين بحركة ذلك الشيء، وإذا كانت المسافة قليلة القدر لا يميز البصر بين الإدراكين فتحسبه النفس ساكنا. أما راكب السفينة فلما لم يدرك لبدنه انتقالا من موضع إلى موضع حسبه ساكنا، وإذا تبدلت محاذاته لأجزاء الشط مع تخيل سكونه في نفسه حسب الشط متحركا لكون ذلك التبدل شبيها بالتبدل الأول. (٢) أقول: ليسكن السائر إلى جهة ينتقل من ا إلى ب والقمر بالقياس إليه مثلج [؟] والغيم المتوسط بينهما الذي لا يحجب القمر لوقته مثل ده فإذا كان المسائر عند ا كان شعاعه الممتد الذي به يرى القمر كخط أزح وإذا انتقل إلى ب صار شعاعه كخط بحد فيتخيل أن القمر تحرك من ز إلى ح في جهة حركته إذراء [؟] أولا محاذيا لنقطة ز ثم منتقلا منها إلى ح. وأما القمر المتحرك إلى خلاف تلك الجهة فلا يحس بحركته السائر [؟]. وأيضا ليكن الناظر ساكنا عند نقطة ورأى القمر وهو ج محاذيا لنقطة ز من الغيم ثم تحرك الغيم في جهة، ووصلت نقطة ح إلى حيث كان في الأول نقطة ز رأى القمر محاذاة نقطة ز إلى محاذاة نقطة ح فيتخيل أن القمر يتحرك من ز إلى ح كحركة الغيم ولا يحس بحركة الغيم لأن انتقاله في المحاذاة بالقياس إلى السماء لا يتغير في حسه لتشابه أجزاء السماء وأجزاء الغيم في الحس. وإذا كان الغيم مثل ح فقط والناظر عند أ رأى القمر بعيدا من طرف الغيم بقدر زح ثم تحرك الغيم إلى أن وصل مبدأه وهو نقطة ح إلى الموضع الذي كان فيه ز رأى القمر ح فيتخيل أن القمر متحرك من ز إلى ح فسار إلى جهة الغيم وهو خلاف جهة حركة الغيم.
Moving and the moving ship is stationary (1) And we may see a moving thing towards a direction moving towards its opposite direction, because the moving thing towards a direction sees the planet moving towards it if it is observed beneath it, and if the planet is moving opposite that direction. And we may see the moon as if moving towards the cloud even if it is moving opposite that direction when the cloud is moving towards it (2). I say: motion is not visible, and when the sight perceives a thing in a position aligned with something after having perceived it in another position aligned with something else, the soul judges by the combination of the two perceptions that the thing is moving. And if the distance is small, the sight does not distinguish between the two perceptions, so the soul considers it stationary. As for the ship passenger, since he does not perceive a transfer of his body from place to place, he considers himself stationary. And when his alignments with parts of the shore change while imagining his own stillness, he considers the shore moving because that change resembles the first change. (2) I say: Suppose the observer moving towards a direction moves from A to B, and the moon relative to him is like [?], and the cloud between them that does not hide the moon at that time is like D E. So when the observer was at A, his extended ray by which he sees the moon was like line A Z H, and when he moved to B, his ray became like line B H D, so it is imagined that the moon moved from Z to H in the direction of his movement, [?] first aligned with point Z then moving from it to H. As for the moon moving opposite that direction, the observer does not perceive its movement. Also, let the observer be stationary at a point, and he sees the moon (which is J) aligned with point Z of the cloud, then the cloud moves in a direction, and point H reaches where point Z was first. He sees the moon's alignment from point Z to point H, so he imagines that the moon moves from Z to H like the cloud's movement, and he does not perceive the cloud's movement because its change in alignment relative to the sky does not change in his sense due to the similarity of the parts of the sky and the parts of the cloud in sensation. And if the cloud is only like H, and the observer at A sees the moon far from the edge of the cloud by the amount Z H, then the cloud moves until its beginning (point H) reaches the position where Z was, he sees the moon at H, so he imagines that the moon moved from Z to H and thus moved towards the cloud, which is opposite the direction of the cloud's movement.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines sensory error in motion perception. It begins by stating the paradoxical phenomenon: a moving ship appears stationary to its passenger (the 'moving stationary'), and we can see something moving in one direction as if moving in the opposite direction. Two examples are given: a planet seen through moving clouds, and the moon appearing to move towards clouds. The author (Imam Razi) then explains the cause: motion itself is not directly visible; rather, the soul judges motion by comparing two successive perceptions of an object's alignment with reference points. If the object is perceived at two different positions relative to something else, the soul infers motion. When the distance is very small, the perceptions blend, and the soul judges stillness. The ship passenger does not perceive his own bodily transfer, so he feels stationary, but the changing alignment with the shore leads him to imagine the shore moving. Similarly, for the moon and clouds, the author provides a geometric explanation: the relative movement of the observer or the clouds changes the alignment of the visual ray, causing the illusion that the moon moves in a different direction. The passage concludes by emphasizing that the senses can be deceived in judging motion, laying the groundwork for the argument that sense perception is not reliable for establishing the continuity or persistence of objects.

Correction: The passage shows that what we perceive can be the opposite of reality.

Key Takeaway
The senses can be deceived: an observer on a moving ship feels stationary and sees the shore moving, and the moon can appear to move towards clouds despite moving away.

?
You see the moon moving opposite its actual direction due to the cloud. In what areas of your life might you be misreading the 'direction' of events—seeing hardship as punishment when it may be mercy, or ease as blessing when it may be a test? Commit now to seek the true direction from Allah before trusting your sight.

How the Soul Perceives Motion
Razi explains the mechanism of motion perception and applies it to the ship illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that motion is not directly perceived but inferred by the soul from two successive perceptions.

Core Idea
Motion is not visible; the soul judges motion by comparing alignments.

After presenting the illusions, Razi explains the underlying principle. Motion itself is not seen; rather, the soul deduces motion from a change in alignment between the object and its surroundings.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation
Read the passage where Razi states 'I say: motion is not visible' and the following lines. Understand the mechanism.
This is the core of the lesson.
2
Understand the role of small distances
Notice that when the distance between two perceptions is small, the sight does not distinguish, so the soul judges stillness.
This explains why gradual changes might go unnoticed.
3
Apply to the ship example
See how the principle explains why the ship passenger feels stationary and sees the shore moving.
The passenger does not perceive his own transfer, so he imagines the shore moving instead.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متحركا والسفينة المتحركة ساكنة (۱) وقد نرى المتحرك إلى جهة متحركا إلى ضد تلك الجهة فإن المتحرك إلى جهة يرى الكوكب متحركا إليها إذا شاهد فيما تحته وإن كان الكوكب متحركا إلى خلاف تلك الجهة، وقد نرى القمر كالسائر إلى الغيم وإن كان سائرا إلى خلاف تلك الجهة إذا كان الغيم سائرا إليه (۲). أقول: الحركة ليست بمرئية، والبصر إذا أدرك الشيء في موضع محازيا لشيء ما بعد أن أدركه في موضع آخر محاذيا لغير ذلك الشيء حكمت النفس عند مجموع الإدراكين بحركة ذلك الشيء، وإذا كانت المسافة قليلة القدر لا يميز البصر بين الإدراكين فتحسبه النفس ساكنا. أما راكب السفينة فلما لم يدرك لبدنه انتقالا من موضع إلى موضع حسبه ساكنا، وإذا تبدلت محاذاته لأجزاء الشط مع تخيل سكونه في نفسه حسب الشط متحركا لكون ذلك التبدل شبيها بالتبدل الأول. (٢) أقول: ليسكن السائر إلى جهة ينتقل من ا إلى ب والقمر بالقياس إليه مثلج [؟] والغيم المتوسط بينهما الذي لا يحجب القمر لوقته مثل ده فإذا كان المسائر عند ا كان شعاعه الممتد الذي به يرى القمر كخط أزح وإذا انتقل إلى ب صار شعاعه كخط بحد فيتخيل أن القمر تحرك من ز إلى ح في جهة حركته إذراء [؟] أولا محاذيا لنقطة ز ثم منتقلا منها إلى ح. وأما القمر المتحرك إلى خلاف تلك الجهة فلا يحس بحركته السائر [؟]. وأيضا ليكن الناظر ساكنا عند نقطة ورأى القمر وهو ج محاذيا لنقطة ز من الغيم ثم تحرك الغيم في جهة، ووصلت نقطة ح إلى حيث كان في الأول نقطة ز رأى القمر محاذاة نقطة ز إلى محاذاة نقطة ح فيتخيل أن القمر يتحرك من ز إلى ح كحركة الغيم ولا يحس بحركة الغيم لأن انتقاله في المحاذاة بالقياس إلى السماء لا يتغير في حسه لتشابه أجزاء السماء وأجزاء الغيم في الحس. وإذا كان الغيم مثل ح فقط والناظر عند أ رأى القمر بعيدا من طرف الغيم بقدر زح ثم تحرك الغيم إلى أن وصل مبدأه وهو نقطة ح إلى الموضع الذي كان فيه ز رأى القمر ح فيتخيل أن القمر متحرك من ز إلى ح فسار إلى جهة الغيم وهو خلاف جهة حركة الغيم.
Moving and the moving ship is stationary (1) And we may see a moving thing towards a direction moving towards its opposite direction, because the moving thing towards a direction sees the planet moving towards it if it is observed beneath it, and if the planet is moving opposite that direction. And we may see the moon as if moving towards the cloud even if it is moving opposite that direction when the cloud is moving towards it (2). I say: motion is not visible, and when the sight perceives a thing in a position aligned with something after having perceived it in another position aligned with something else, the soul judges by the combination of the two perceptions that the thing is moving. And if the distance is small, the sight does not distinguish between the two perceptions, so the soul considers it stationary. As for the ship passenger, since he does not perceive a transfer of his body from place to place, he considers himself stationary. And when his alignments with parts of the shore change while imagining his own stillness, he considers the shore moving because that change resembles the first change. (2) I say: Suppose the observer moving towards a direction moves from A to B, and the moon relative to him is like [?], and the cloud between them that does not hide the moon at that time is like D E. So when the observer was at A, his extended ray by which he sees the moon was like line A Z H, and when he moved to B, his ray became like line B H D, so it is imagined that the moon moved from Z to H in the direction of his movement, [?] first aligned with point Z then moving from it to H. As for the moon moving opposite that direction, the observer does not perceive its movement. Also, let the observer be stationary at a point, and he sees the moon (which is J) aligned with point Z of the cloud, then the cloud moves in a direction, and point H reaches where point Z was first. He sees the moon's alignment from point Z to point H, so he imagines that the moon moves from Z to H like the cloud's movement, and he does not perceive the cloud's movement because its change in alignment relative to the sky does not change in his sense due to the similarity of the parts of the sky and the parts of the cloud in sensation. And if the cloud is only like H, and the observer at A sees the moon far from the edge of the cloud by the amount Z H, then the cloud moves until its beginning (point H) reaches the position where Z was, he sees the moon at H, so he imagines that the moon moved from Z to H and thus moved towards the cloud, which is opposite the direction of the cloud's movement.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines sensory error in motion perception. It begins by stating the paradoxical phenomenon: a moving ship appears stationary to its passenger (the 'moving stationary'), and we can see something moving in one direction as if moving in the opposite direction. Two examples are given: a planet seen through moving clouds, and the moon appearing to move towards clouds. The author (Imam Razi) then explains the cause: motion itself is not directly visible; rather, the soul judges motion by comparing two successive perceptions of an object's alignment with reference points. If the object is perceived at two different positions relative to something else, the soul infers motion. When the distance is very small, the perceptions blend, and the soul judges stillness. The ship passenger does not perceive his own bodily transfer, so he feels stationary, but the changing alignment with the shore leads him to imagine the shore moving. Similarly, for the moon and clouds, the author provides a geometric explanation: the relative movement of the observer or the clouds changes the alignment of the visual ray, causing the illusion that the moon moves in a different direction. The passage concludes by emphasizing that the senses can be deceived in judging motion, laying the groundwork for the argument that sense perception is not reliable for establishing the continuity or persistence of objects.

سفينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That motion is directly seen.
Correction: Razi argues that motion is inferred by the soul from two perceptions.

Checkpoint
Why does the soul judge an object as stationary when the distance between two perceptions is small?
A) The object is actually still
B) The sight does not distinguish between the two perceptions
C) The soul is lazy
D) The distance is too far

Key Takeaway
Motion is not directly seen; the soul infers it from a perceived change in alignment. The ship passenger's illusion arises because he does not perceive his own transfer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been absolutely certain about something you perceived, only to later discover you were deceived by your own mind? How does this passage about the ship passenger challenge your trust in your senses and call you to rely on reasoned evidence instead?
?
Are there areas of your faith where you rely on 'direct perception'—like feeling God's presence or judging someone's piety by appearances—rather than careful reasoning from revelation? What would it mean to treat your beliefs like the soul's judgment: built on multiple pieces of evidence rather than immediate impressions?
?
This lesson shows that the soul infers motion by comparing alignments. In your spiritual life, are you comparing yourself to others to gauge your own progress? How can you shift your reference point to Allah’s standards alone, avoiding the illusion that you are moving forward when you are actually still?

The Geometry of the Moon Illusion
Razi provides geometric analysis for the moon and cloud illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the geometric explanation for why the moon can appear to move towards clouds when actually moving away.

Core Idea
The relative motion of the observer or clouds alters the visual ray, creating an illusion of the moon's motion.

This lesson delves into Razi's detailed geometric model for the moon illusion. By tracing the visual ray, he shows how the moon's apparent motion can be opposite to its real motion depending on the movement of the observer or intervening clouds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Identify the points A, B, Z, H, D, E, and J in the geometric model. The observer, moon, and cloud are placed along lines.
The passage uses a diagram or mental image.
2
Trace the first case: observer moves
Follow the ray from the observer at A to the moon, then at B. Note how the alignment changes, making the moon appear to move from Z to H.
This explains the illusion when the observer is moving.
3
Trace the second case: cloud moves
With the observer stationary, the cloud moves. The moon's alignment relative to the cloud changes, creating the illusion that the moon moves with the cloud.
The cloud's own motion is not perceived due to uniform sky.
4
Reflect on the role of reference frames
Consider how the choice of reference (the shore, the cloud) affects perceived motion.
This aligns with modern concepts of relative motion.

Source Text

Mirror Distortions: The Cylindrical Mirror
Razi describes cylindrical mirror distortions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a cylindrical mirror distorts the face, making it appear longer or wider depending on orientation.

Core Idea
A mirror's shape controls reflection, creating predictable distortions.

We have seen that the senses can be mistaken. The cylindrical mirror provides a clear demonstration: when the mirror is straight in one direction and curved in another, it distorts the face along those axes. This passage explains the mechanism of such distortion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the main claim and the example.
Notice the mirror is described as 'like a cylindrical mold'.
2
Identify the two orientations
The passage describes two alignments: mirror's length aligned with face's length, then with face's width. See how the appearance changes.
3
Understand the reflection mechanism
Length reflects from a straight surface, width from a curved surface. This explains why one dimension is preserved and the other compressed.
This is the key insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا نظرنا إلى المرآة رأينا الوجه طويلا وعريضا ومعوجا حسب اختلاف شكل المرآة وكل ذلك يدل على غلط الحس. أقول: المرآة الطويلة المستقيمة في الطول والمنحنية في العرض كقالب أسطوانة مستديرة إذا نظر إليها بحيث يكون طولها محاذيا لطول الوجه يرى الوجه فيها طويلا طوله بقدر طول الوجه قليل العرض لانعكاس الشعاع العرضي بما هو أقل عرضا مما لو كان مستقيما، وذلك لأن الطول ينعكس من عاكس مستقيم، والعرض ينعكس من عاكس منحن، وإذا نظر إليها بحيث يكون طولها محاذيا لعرض الوجه كان الأمر بالعكس، فترى الوجه عريضا عرضه بقدر عرض الوجه وطوله أقل من طوله، وإذا نظر إليها بحيث يكون موريا [؟] في محاذاة الوجه يرى الوجه معوجا، وإذا كانت المرآة بحيث ينعكس منها الشعاع من موضعين أو أكثر إلى موضع واحد رأى الناظر فيها لنفسه وجهين أو أكثر ورأسين أو أكثر، ومن بعضها يرى وجهه منكوسا، وكذلك في الاختلافات المتنوعة التي تشتمل على أكثرها كتب المرايا ويحتال لها متخذو المرايا على وجه يقصدونه، فقد ظهر مما مر أن كل ذلك غلط باد من الإدراك النفساني من المحسوسات المنادية إليها، لا غلط الحس.
And if we look at a mirror, we see the face long, wide, and twisted according to the different shape of the mirror, and all that indicates the error of the sense. I say: The mirror that is long, straight in length, and curved in width, like a cylindrical mold, if one looks into it such that its length is aligned with the length of the face, the face appears long, its length equal to the length of the face, but narrow in width, because the transverse ray reflects from what is less wide than if it were straight. That is because the length reflects from a straight reflector, and the width reflects from a curved reflector. And if one looks into it such that its length is aligned with the width of the face, the opposite occurs, so you see the face wide, its width equal to the width of the face, and its length less than its length. And if one looks into it such that it is [?] aligned with the face, the face is seen twisted. And if the mirror is such that the ray reflects from two or more positions to one position, the observer sees in it two or more faces and two or more heads, and in some mirrors he sees his face inverted. And similarly for the various differences, most of which are contained in the books on mirrors, and the makers of mirrors contrive them intentionally. So it has become clear from what has passed that all that is an error apparent from the psychic perception of the sensible things that are referred to, not an error of the sense itself.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage explains how mirrors can distort the image of the face, further demonstrating sensory illusion. The author first states the phenomenon: depending on the mirror's shape, the face appears long, wide, or twisted. This shows that the sense of sight can be mistaken. He then gives detailed examples. For a long mirror that is straight in length but curved in width (like a cylindrical segment), if the mirror's length aligns with the face's length, the face appears elongated—because the length is reflected from a straight surface (which preserves proportions) while the width is reflected from a curved surface (which compresses it). Conversely, if the mirror is rotated so that its length aligns with the face's width, the face appears wider and shorter. If the mirror is placed obliquely, the image appears twisted. The author also notes that concave or multiple-reflection mirrors can produce multiple faces or inverted images. He concludes that these are not errors of the sense itself but of the psychic perception (al-idrak al-nafsani) that interprets the sensory data. This distinction is important: the senses provide raw data, but the soul's interpretation can be mistaken.

مرآة
انعكاس
شعاع
طول
عرض

Checkpoint
Why does the face appear narrow when looking into a cylindrical mirror aligned with the face's length?
A) Because the mirror is longer than the face.

C) Because the face is actually narrow.
D) Because the mirror is curved in length.

Key Takeaway
A cylindrical mirror distorts the face by reflecting length and width differently, proving that the sense of sight can be misled by the shape of the reflector.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If a simple mirror can deceive your eyes so predictably, what other 'mirrors' – desires, habits, assumptions – might distort your spiritual sight? What would it take to find a straight reflector that shows you the truth?
?
Razi shows that sensory error is not random but follows a pattern. What patterns of self-deception have you noticed in your life, and what does this teach you about the need to submit your perceptions to a higher standard of truth?

Other Mirror Distortions: Twisted, Multiple, and Inverted
Razi describes twisted, multiple, and inverted mirror illusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how mirrors can produce twisted, multiple, or inverted images, and why these are errors of interpretation, not the senses.

Core Idea
Multiple reflections reveal the soul's role in interpreting sensory data.

The same principle—mirror shape affects reflection—can produce even more striking illusions. Twisted images, multiple faces, and inverted heads are all possible. This passage shows that these are not failures of the eyes but of the psychic perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the additional distortions described.
Notice the variety: twisted, multiple, inverted.
2
Identify the three types
The passage mentions twisted images (oblique alignment), multiple faces (two or more reflections), and inverted faces.
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that all these distortions are errors of psychic perception, not of the sense itself.
This is the key takeaway.

وإذا نظرنا إلى المرآة رأينا الوجه طويلا وعريضا ومعوجا حسب اختلاف شكل المرآة وكل ذلك يدل على غلط الحس. أقول: المرآة الطويلة المستقيمة في الطول والمنحنية في العرض كقالب أسطوانة مستديرة إذا نظر إليها بحيث يكون طولها محاذيا لطول الوجه يرى الوجه فيها طويلا طوله بقدر طول الوجه قليل العرض لانعكاس الشعاع العرضي بما هو أقل عرضا مما لو كان مستقيما، وذلك لأن الطول ينعكس من عاكس مستقيم، والعرض ينعكس من عاكس منحن، وإذا نظر إليها بحيث يكون طولها محاذيا لعرض الوجه كان الأمر بالعكس، فترى الوجه عريضا عرضه بقدر عرض الوجه وطوله أقل من طوله، وإذا نظر إليها بحيث يكون موريا [؟] في محاذاة الوجه يرى الوجه معوجا، وإذا كانت المرآة بحيث ينعكس منها الشعاع من موضعين أو أكثر إلى موضع واحد رأى الناظر فيها لنفسه وجهين أو أكثر ورأسين أو أكثر، ومن بعضها يرى وجهه منكوسا، وكذلك في الاختلافات المتنوعة التي تشتمل على أكثرها كتب المرايا ويحتال لها متخذو المرايا على وجه يقصدونه، فقد ظهر مما مر أن كل ذلك غلط باد من الإدراك النفساني من المحسوسات المنادية إليها، لا غلط الحس.
And if we look at a mirror, we see the face long, wide, and twisted according to the different shape of the mirror, and all that indicates the error of the sense. I say: The mirror that is long, straight in length, and curved in width, like a cylindrical mold, if one looks into it such that its length is aligned with the length of the face, the face appears long, its length equal to the length of the face, but narrow in width, because the transverse ray reflects from what is less wide than if it were straight. That is because the length reflects from a straight reflector, and the width reflects from a curved reflector. And if one looks into it such that its length is aligned with the width of the face, the opposite occurs, so you see the face wide, its width equal to the width of the face, and its length less than its length. And if one looks into it such that it is [?] aligned with the face, the face is seen twisted. And if the mirror is such that the ray reflects from two or more positions to one position, the observer sees in it two or more faces and two or more heads, and in some mirrors he sees his face inverted. And similarly for the various differences, most of which are contained in the books on mirrors, and the makers of mirrors contrive them intentionally. So it has become clear from what has passed that all that is an error apparent from the psychic perception of the sensible things that are referred to, not an error of the sense itself.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage explains how mirrors can distort the image of the face, further demonstrating sensory illusion. The author first states the phenomenon: depending on the mirror's shape, the face appears long, wide, or twisted. This shows that the sense of sight can be mistaken. He then gives detailed examples. For a long mirror that is straight in length but curved in width (like a cylindrical segment), if the mirror's length aligns with the face's length, the face appears elongated—because the length is reflected from a straight surface (which preserves proportions) while the width is reflected from a curved surface (which compresses it). Conversely, if the mirror is rotated so that its length aligns with the face's width, the face appears wider and shorter. If the mirror is placed obliquely, the image appears twisted. The author also notes that concave or multiple-reflection mirrors can produce multiple faces or inverted images. He concludes that these are not errors of the sense itself but of the psychic perception (al-idrak al-nafsani) that interprets the sensory data. This distinction is important: the senses provide raw data, but the soul's interpretation can be mistaken.

Key Terms
مرآة
انعكاس
شعاع
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mirrors distort the face because the sense of sight is inherently unreliable.
Correction: The sense of sight provides accurate raw data, but the soul misinterprets the reflected image due to the mirror's shape. The error is in psychic perception, not in the sense.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a mirror produce multiple images of the face?
A) Because the mirror is broken.
B) Because the ray reflects from two or more positions to one point.
C) Because the face is moving.
D) Because the mirror is curved.

Key Takeaway
Mirrors can produce twisted, multiple, or inverted images, but these are errors of interpretation, not of the senses themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you misinterpret someone's facial expression, do you blame your eyes or your heart? How can Razi's insight help you take responsibility for your judgments?
?
Are there areas in your life where you insist on being 'right' based on your first impressions? Will you now pause to check if your soul is distorting the data?
?
You see a twisted image in a mirror and know it's not real. But when your soul twists someone's words or actions, why do you believe the distortion immediately? What would it take for you to question your own psychic perception?

Sense Misjudges Continuity of the Similar
Razi presents the second argument against sense perception, focusing on continuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the sense's judgment of continuity is unreliable due to its inability to distinguish between a thing and its exact likeness.

Core Idea
The sense mistakes a series of similar things for one continuous thing.

Imam Razi continues his critique of sense perception by focusing on how we perceive continuity. He argues that when similar things follow one another rapidly, the senses cannot tell them apart and treat them as a single enduring object. This sets the stage for why rational arguments, not sensory experience, are needed to establish divine attributes like permanence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on continuity
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note how Razi states his claim and then supports it with two parts: a general reason and a specific example.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the sentence where Razi explains why the sense misjudges continuity: 'because the sense does not distinguish between a thing and its similar, and therefore confusion occurs.'
This is the key logical step.
3
Examine the example of colors
Notice how Razi applies the argument to colors, citing the Ash'ari view that colors are created moment by moment yet appear continuous. This shows the sense can be deceived even in a common experience.
The example is used to generalize to bodies.
4
Consider the implication for bodies
Razi extends the argument to bodies: if similar bodies are renewed, the sense would mistake them for one. This undermines trust in sensory judgments of permanence.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that the sense's judgment of continuity is unacceptable. Ask yourself: what does this mean for using sense experience as proof in theological arguments?
This connects to the broader theme of the work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : أن الحس قد يجزم بالاستمرار على الشيء مع أنه لا يكون كذلك ، لأن الحس لا يفرق بين الشيء ومثله ولذلك يحصل الالتباس بين الشيء ومثله ، فبتقدير توالى الأمثال يظن الحس وجوداً واحداً مستمراً ولا يكون كذلك ؛ فإن الألوان غير باقية عند أهل السنة ، بل يحدثها الله تعالى حالا فالا ، مع أن البصر يحكم بوجود لون واحد مستمر ، وإذا احتمل ذلك ، احتمل أيضاً أن يقال : الأجسام لا تبقى مستمرة ، بل الله تعالى مجددها حالا فالا ، لكنها ما كانت متماثلة متوالية يظنها الحس شيئاً واحدا فثبت أن حكم الحس بالبقاء غير مقبول. ثم يبين ذلك بالمرآة: وإلا فينعكس من نقطة ويكون الشعاع المنفذ من أ إلى ر منعكسا عنه إلى ح وحينئذ يجب أن يكون زاوية أرب الخارجة عن مثلث او ه أعظم من زاوية ا . ب لكن زاوية ارب مساوية لزاوية حد د وزاوية ادب مساوية الزاوية . د فزاوية حدد أعظم من زاوية ى . د ی ويكون أعظم كثيرا من زاوية ح ه د فالداخلة في مثلث ى ره أعظم من خارجتها هذا خلف محال ، ولا يمكن أن ينعكس من ، شعاع إلى جزء أسفل من رأس الشجر كنقطة وإلا كانت زاوية ا . ب مساوية لكل واحد من زاويتي ح یه دا ح . د العظمى والصغرى هذا خلف، فإذاً لابد من أن ينعكس إلى كل نقطة تميل من الرأس إلى أسفل من نقطة تكون من . إلى د أميل حتى تتصل القاعدة بالقاعدة ، ولما كانت النفس لا تدرك الانعكاس فإنها متعودة لرؤية المرئيات بنفوذ الشعاع على الاستقامة بحسب الشعاع المنعكس نافذا في الماء ، ولا يكون في نفس الأمر نافذا فإن الماء وبما لا يكون عميقا بقدر طول الشجر ، أو يكون كدرا لا ينفذ فيه الشعاع أصلا، وحينئذ يحسب أن رأس الشجر أكثر نزولا فى الماء لكونه أبعد من أصله وباقي أجزائه على الترتيب ، فرآه كأنه متنكس تحت سطح الماء ، وهذه المسائل وإن كانت متعلقة بالهندسة أوردناما مهنا لان الكلام انجر إليها .
And the second [argument]: that the sense may affirm continuity of a thing while it is not so, because the sense does not distinguish between a thing and its similar, and therefore confusion occurs between a thing and its similar. So if similar things follow one another, the sense supposes a single continuous existence, but it is not so. For colors are not permanent (baqiya) according to the People of the Sunna (ahl al-sunna); rather, Allah creates them moment by moment, while the eye judges the existence of a single continuous color. And if that is possible, it is also possible to say: bodies do not persist continuously; rather, Allah renews them moment by moment, but since they are similar and successive, the sense supposes them to be one thing. So it is established that the sense's judgment of continuity (al-baqa') is unacceptable. Then this is explained by the mirror: otherwise it would reflect from a point, and the ray passing from A to R would be reflected from it to H, and then the angle ARB external to triangle O H would be greater than angle A B, but angle ARB equals angle H D D and angle ADB equals angle D, so angle H D D is greater than angle Y D Y and becomes much greater than angle H H D, so the interior in triangle Y R H is greater than its exterior—this is a false contradiction. It is impossible for a ray to reflect to a part lower than the top of the tree like a point; otherwise angle A B would equal each of the two angles H Y H D A H D, the greater and the smaller—this is a contradiction. So it must reflect to every point that inclines from the top downward from a point that is from... to D more inclined until the base connects with the base. And since the soul does not perceive the reflection, it is accustomed to seeing visible things by the rectilinear penetration of the ray, according to the reflected ray penetrating into water, while in reality it is not penetrating because the water is not as deep as the length of the tree, or it is turbid so the ray does not penetrate at all. Then it reckons that the top of the tree is more descended in the water because it is farther from its root, and its remaining parts in order, so it sees it as if inverted under the surface of the water. And these issues, although they belong to geometry, we have mentioned them here because the discourse has led to them.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the reliability of sense perception. Imam Razi begins by stating that the senses can judge continuity of a thing when in fact it is not continuous, because the senses cannot distinguish between a thing and its exact likeness. When similar things occur in succession, the senses perceive them as one continuous existence. He gives the example of colors: according to the Ash'aris, colors are not permanent but are created by Allah moment by moment; yet the eye sees a single continuous color. This possibility implies that the sense's judgment of continuity (al-baqa') is not trustworthy. Then Razi provides a geometrical demonstration involving reflection in a mirror: he shows that due to the laws of reflection, the ray paths lead to contradictions if we assume direct perception, proving that the sense can be deceived—specifically in the case of seeing a tree reflected in water, where the soul misinterprets the reflected image as a direct one, leading to the illusion of an inverted tree. The passage concludes by noting that these geometrical details are brought in because the discussion necessitates them.

Key Terms
غلط الحس
الاستمرار
توالى الأمثال
الألوان غير باقية
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that the senses are always wrong.
Correction: Razi only argues that the sense is unreliable for judging continuity of similar successive things, not that it is always mistaken.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi argue that the sense's judgment of continuity is unreliable?
A) Because the senses are inherently flawed and never correct.
B) Because the senses cannot distinguish between a thing and its exact likeness, so when similar things follow, a single continuous existence is assumed.
C) Because geometry proves all sense perception is false.
D) Because the Ash'aris say colors are permanent, contradicting the sense.

Key Takeaway
The sense's judgment of continuity is unreliable because it confuses a series of similar things with one continuous thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed someone's character or your own spirituality was unchanging, only to realize it was shifting moment by moment? How might this argument change how you evaluate your own faith and repentance?
?
This lesson shows that your senses can trick you into perceiving something as continuous when it is actually being recreated each instant. What specific habit or assumption in your life might be built on such an illusion? How will you investigate it starting today?
?
If your sense of 'being the same person' moment to moment is not trustworthy, what does that mean for your accountability before Allah? Are you willing to see each moment as a fresh creation and a fresh opportunity to obey?

The Mirror Demonstration: Geometry Exposes Sense Error
Razi provides a geometrical mirror demonstration to illustrate sense deception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses a geometrical mirror reflection argument to demonstrate that sense perception can be deceived, reinforcing the unreliability of sensory evidence.

Core Idea
Geometry shows that reflective surfaces can fool the sense into seeing an inverted image.

After presenting the general argument about continuity, Imam Razi provides a detailed geometrical example involving a mirror to further illustrate the fallibility of the senses. He shows that the laws of reflection lead to contradictions if we assume direct perception, and then explains how the soul misinterprets reflected rays as direct, leading to the illusion of an inverted tree in water.

Guided Path
1
Read the mirror passage
Read the Arabic text and translation from 'ثم يبين ذلك بالمرآة' to the end. This passage is dense and geometrical.
Focus on the overall point rather than every geometric detail.
2
Understand the geometric contradiction
Razi sets up a hypothetical scenario where a ray reflects in a way that would lead to impossible angles. This proves that the sense's direct perception assumption is false.
The contradiction shows that the sense cannot be trusted in this case.
3
Relate to the tree-in-water illusion
Razi applies the geometry to the common illusion of a tree reflected in water. The soul, unfamiliar with reflection, interprets the reflected rays as coming straight from the tree, causing the tree to appear inverted.
4
Consider the conclusion
Razi notes that these geometric details are mentioned because the discussion leads to them. The takeaway is that sense perception can be systematically deceived.
5
Connect to the first argument
Reflect on how this mirror example supports the earlier claim about continuity: both show that the senses are fallible in specific ways.

Source Text

?

?

?

The Dream Argument Against Sense Certainty
Imam Razi presents the dream argument against sense certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the phenomenon of dreaming to challenge the absolute certainty of wakeful sense perception.

Core Idea
Dreams show that what seems certain can be false — so wakeful certainty may also be relative.

Imam Razi presents a powerful skeptical argument: we have all experienced vivid dreams that felt completely real at the time, only to discover they were false upon waking. If one state of consciousness can be deceived relative to another, why couldn't our current wakeful state also be deceived relative to a higher state? This passage introduces that unsettling possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the logical flow: observation of dream deception, then an analogy to wakefulness.
The argument is short but dense; pay attention to the conditional 'if that is possible, then why not...'
2
Identify the key components
Identify the sleeper, the dream state, wakefulness, and the hypothetical third state. Understand how each part functions.
The third state is not defined; it is a logical possibility.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument challenges the reliability of sense perception. Does it prove wakeful experience is false?
The argument only suggests fallibility, not actual falsehood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها : أن النائم يرى فى النوم شيئاً ويجزم بثبوته ، ثم يتبين له في اليقظة أن ذلك الجزم كان باطلا ، وإذا جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يكون هنا حالة ثالثة يظهر لنا فيها كذب ما رأيناه في اليقظة (٢)
And the third [argument]: that the sleeper sees something in sleep and affirms its existence, then it becomes clear to him in wakefulness that that affirmation was false. And if that is possible, then why is it not possible that there is a third state in which the falsity of what we saw in wakefulness becomes apparent to us?
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the fallibility of sense-based knowledge. Imam Razi points out that during sleep, a person sees things and is certain of their reality, but upon waking, they realize those perceptions were false. If such deceptions occur in one state (sleep) relative to another (wakefulness), it is logically possible that there exists a third state (perhaps a higher level of consciousness or a divine unveiling) relative to which even our wakeful perceptions are false. Thus, our ordinary experience cannot be taken as absolutely certain.

Key Terms
النائم
النوم
اليقظة
حالة ثالثة
الجزم الباطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dream argument proves that all wakeful perceptions are false.
Correction: The argument only shows that it is possible for wakeful perceptions to be false from a higher perspective, not that they are actually false.

Checkpoint
What is the third argument's analogy for the fallibility of wakeful perception?
A) Colors change in different lights.
B) Dreams are later known to be false, suggesting wakeful experience might also be false from a higher perspective.
C) Mirrors produce distorted images.
D) The blind do not see.

Key Takeaway
The fact that dreams feel real yet turn out false opens the possibility that our waking experience may also be relative to a higher state of consciousness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If the 'third state' were to expose that certainty as illusion today, would you be willing to reconsider it? What would it take for you to honestly examine it this week?
?
If you knew with certainty that the afterlife is that 'third state' where all worldly certainties are reversed, how would you live differently starting now? Name one specific habit or priority that you would change if you truly believed that today's sure things might be tomorrow's illusions. Commit to that change today.

Fourth Argument: Pleurisy Patient's Hallucinations
Imam Razi presents the fourth argument: the case of a pleurisy patient seeing non-existent things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of a pleurisy patient's hallucinations serves to challenge the reliability of sense perception.

Core Idea
A sick person's certainty about hallucinations shows that perception can be deceived.

Imam Razi continues his critique of sensory knowledge by presenting a vivid case: a patient with pleurisy hallucinates terrifying forms that do not exist, yet is fully convinced of their reality. This powerful example forces us to question whether even the perceptions of healthy people are immune to error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: premise (the patient sees non-existent forms), observation (he is certain and screams), conclusion (this shows a state of seeing non-existents as existent).
The passage uses a simple conditional: 'if that is possible, then why not in health?'
2
Understand the argument's logic
The argument is an analogy: if a sick person can be deceived, then it is possible that healthy people are also deceived. Razi does not claim they always are, but opens the door to doubt.
This is a skeptical move, not a denial of all perception.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument fits into the broader critique of sense perception. It shows that certainty does not guarantee truth, because the patient is certain yet wrong.
This challenges the foundational role of sense perception in acquiring knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage presents the fourth argument against the reliability of sense perception. This shows that a person can be in a state where he perceives nonexistent things as existent. If such a state can occur in sickness, then by analogy, it is possible that healthy people likewise perceive things that do not exist. Thus, the evidence of the senses is not absolutely reliable.

Objection and Answer on Disease as Cause
Objection and answer: disease as cause of hallucination, impossibility of certainty without exhaustive cause enumeration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi responds to the objection that only disease causes hallucination, and why this argument leads to the impossibility of absolute certainty.

Core Idea
Absence of one cause does not guarantee absence of effect without exhaustive enumeration.

This passage presents a classic dialectical move in kalam: an objector raises a specific cause (disease) for hallucination, and the responder argues that the absence of that particular cause does not rule out other possible causes. To achieve certainty about reality, one would need to know all possible causes of error and prove their absence—a task requiring precise reasoning that may itself be uncertain.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and identify the claim
Read the first sentence (the objection): the objector states that the cause of the hallucinatory state is disease, so in health it does not occur. Note the assumption that disease is the sole cause.
2
Read the answer and note the logical structure
Read the responder's answer (قلت). Identify the key point: absence of one cause does not entail absence of the judgment. Then follow the reasoning: to rule out the possibility, one must enumerate all causes of false imagination and prove their absence.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يوجب انتفاء الحكم'.
3
Understand the further conditions
The responder adds that the effect cannot occur or persist without its causes. Then he states that establishing each of these premises requires precise examination (النظر الدقيق), which may not be possible.
This elevates the argument from a simple counterexample to an epistemological challenge.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that one cannot be certain of the existence of anything (لا يجوز الجزم بوجود شيء). Reflect on why this follows: because we cannot exhaustively rule out all potential causes of error.
This is a skeptical conclusion regarding empirical certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : الموجب لتلك الحالة هو المرض فعند الصحة لا يوجد. قلت : انتفاء السبب الواحد لا يوجب انتفاء الحكم بل هذا الاحتمال لا يندفع إلا بحصر أسباب ذلك التخيل الكاذب ثم بيان انتفائها ، ثم إن المسبب لا يجوز حصوله ولا بقاؤه عند انتفاء الأسباب لكن كل واحدة من هذه المقدمات مما لا يمكن إثباته إلا بالنظر الدقيق لو أمكن، فيلزم أن لا يجوز الجزم بوجود شيء
For if you say: The cause of that state is disease, so in health it does not occur. I say: The absence of one cause does not entail the absence of the judgment; rather, this possibility is not repelled except by enumerating all the causes of that false imagination and then showing their absence. Moreover, the effect cannot occur nor persist in the absence of the causes. But every one of these premises cannot be established except by precise examination, if possible. So it follows that it is not permissible to be certain of the existence of anything.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector claims that the hallucinatory state is caused specifically by disease, so healthy people are not subject to such errors. The response (قلت) argues that the absence of one particular cause (disease) does not prove the absence of the effect (false perception) entirely, because there might be other causes. To rule out the possibility of error in healthy people, one would need to enumerate all possible causes of false imagination and then prove that none exist in the healthy state. Furthermore, the effect cannot occur or persist without its causes, but establishing all these premises requires precise rational examination (النظر الدقيق), which is itself uncertain. Therefore, the argument concludes that one cannot attain absolute certainty about the existence of any perceived thing based on sense experience alone.

Key Terms
الموجب
المرض
انتفاء السبب
حصر أسباب التخيل الكاذب
النظر الدقيق
الجزم بوجود شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer claims that healthy people never hallucinate because the cause (disease) is absent.
Correction: The answer actually argues that absence of one cause does not rule out other causes, so healthy people could still hallucinate from other causes. The point is that certainty requires exhaustive knowledge which is unattainable.

Checkpoint
How does the answer respond to the objection that only sick people hallucinate?
A) By admitting that healthy people never hallucinate.
B) By arguing that the absence of one cause does not rule out other possible causes of error.
C) By claiming that healthy people are also sick.
D) By denying that hallucination ever occurs.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of exhaustive enumeration of all possible causes of error undermines certainty in empirical perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Permanence Is Judged by Intellect, Not Sense
Al-Razi argues permanence judgment is intellectual, not sensory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the judgment of permanence (that something remains identical over time) belongs to the intellect and cannot be made by sense perception.

Core Idea
Only intellect can declare identity across time; sense lacks the capacity.

This passage presents a foundational epistemological claim: that judging whether something persists as the same entity from one moment to the next is an intellectual operation, not a sensory one. Imam al-Razi explains why sense cannot make this judgment and then traces how three schools—Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and philosophers—derive their positions from rational principles, not sense. Understanding this distinction sharpens your grasp of how kalam arguments about divine attributes and created things are grounded in rational analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage through once
Read the Arabic and translation without stopping. Notice how al-Razi first defines permanence, then argues against sense, and then discusses three schools.
The passage is dense; a first read gives you the overall arc.
2
Understand the definition of permanence
Focus on the first sentence: permanence means the existent in the second moment is the very same as in the first moment. This is the concept being judged.
3
See why sense cannot judge permanence
Al-Razi says sense cannot summon two different times simultaneously. Since permanence requires comparing moments, sense fails. This is the core argument.
Sense only perceives the present instant.
4
Examine the schools' applications
The Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and philosophers each have rational principles that lead to different conclusions about the permanence of accidents and bodies. Al-Razi shows these are all intellectual, not sensory.
This illustrates how diverse rational conclusions arise from different axioms.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
The judgment of permanence is always rational. Sensory error is actually intellectual error when the intellect fails to differentiate similar things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : الحكم بالبقاء هو الحكم بأن الموجود في الزمان الثاني هو بعينه الموجود في الزمان الأول ، وهذا الحكم لا يصح من الحس، فإنه لا يقدر على استحضار الزمانين فكيف يستحضر الموجود فيهما ، فإذا الحكم بالبقاء لا يكون إلا من العقل والعقل إنما يغلط إذا عقل المشترك بين الشيئين المتشاب بين ولم يعقل ما به يمتاز كل واحد منهما عن الآخر ، فإحالة هذا الغلط على الحمس ليس بصواب، وأما حكم الأشاعرة بأن الألوان غير باقية فشيء لزمهم بحسب أصولهم المسلمة عندهم وهى أن الإعدام لا يمكن أن يكون فعلا الفاعل ، وأن الموجود الباقي حال بقائه مستغن عن المؤثر ، وأن لا مؤثر إلا الله تعالى، وإذا شاهدوا أعراضا لايدوم وجودها ألزموا القول بتجددها حالا بعد حال. والمعتزلة لما جوزوا طريان الضد على محل الضد الآخر المقتضى لإفتائه لم يقولوا بذلك ، والفلاسفة لما جعلوا البرقى حال بقائه محتاجا إلى امؤثر لم يحتاجوا إلى ارتكاب ذلك ، والعظام من المعتزلة جمل الأجسام أيضاً غير باقية بمثل ذلك ، وهذه أحكام غير متعلقة بالحس .
I say: The judgment of permanence is the judgment that the existent in the second moment is the very same as the existent in the first moment. This judgment is not valid from sense, for sense cannot summon the two moments, so how can it summon the existent in them? Therefore the judgment of permanence comes only from the intellect. And the intellect only errs when it grasps the commonality between two similar things and does not grasp what distinguishes each from the other. So attributing this error to sense is not correct. As for the Ash'aris' judgment that colors are not permanent, that is something necessitated by their accepted principles: that non-existence cannot be an act of the agent, that the existent thing while remaining is independent of the effective cause, and that there is no effective cause but God. When they observed accidents that do not persist in existence, they were compelled to say that they are renewed moment by moment. The Mu'tazila, since they allowed the occurrence of a contrary to the locus of another contrary necessitating its annihilation, did not say this. The philosophers, since they held that the Creator while remaining needs no effective cause, did not need to resort to that. And the great ones of the Mu'tazila made bodies also not permanent in a similar manner. These are judgments not related to sense.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained argument about the nature of the judgment of permanence (al-ḥukm bi al-baqāʾ). The author, Imam al-Razi (or his glossator), begins by defining the judgment of permanence as the judgment that an entity existing at a second time is numerically identical to what existed at the first time. He argues that such a judgment cannot be made by sense perception because sense cannot bring together two different times to compare them; it only perceives the present moment. Hence, the judgment of permanence is necessarily an intellectual judgment. Then he cautions that the intellect errs only when it apprehends the common aspect between two similar things without noting the difference—so such error is intellectual, not sensory, and therefore cannot be cited to discredit sense perception. The author then surveys three theological schools: the Ash'aris, who hold that accidents like colors are not permanent (non-enduring) because their principles require that non-existence cannot be an act of God, that an existing thing while persisting is independent of an effector, and that only God is the effector; so they say accidents are renewed at each moment. The Mu'tazila, by contrast, allowed the occurrence of a contrary in the locus of another contrary, which terminates the first, so they did not need to deny permanence of accidents. The philosophers held that the Creator remains without needing an effector, so they too avoided that position. Some prominent Mu'tazila even extended non-permanence to bodies. The author concludes that all these judgments are not based on sense but on rational principles.

Key Terms
الحكم بالبقاء
الحس
العقل
الأشاعرة
المعتزلة
الفلاسفة
الأعراض
الأجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that sense perception can judge whether something persists over time (e.g., seeing an object now and assuming it is the same as before).
Correction: Sense perceives only the present moment; comparing across time requires intellect, as argued by al-Razi.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot sense perception judge permanence?
A) Because sense perception is always deceptive.
B) Because sense cannot simultaneously perceive two different times.
C) Because the intellect forbids sense from making such judgments.
D) Because permanence is a non-sensory attribute.

Key Takeaway
Judgment of permanence is intellectual, not sensory; the intellect's errors come from failing to distinguish similar things, not from sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own spiritual state 'persisting' over time, do you typically rely on fleeting feelings and sensory experiences, or on the steady reflection of your intellect? How might this lesson challenge you to trust your intellect more than your senses in matters of faith?

?
The intellect errs when it fails to distinguish what is common from what is distinct. In your relationship with God, where might you be conflating similar but different acts (e.g., acts of worship for approval of people vs. for Allah alone)? What step will you take today to sharpen that distinction?

Refuting the Dreamer Argument Against Sense Perception
Al-Razi refutes the skeptics' objection from dream experience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi refutes the skeptical argument from dreaming by distinguishing between sensory perception and judgment error.

Core Idea
Dreams don't disprove the senses; the error is in the sleeper's judgment, not in sensation.

One of the strongest skeptical objections against sense perception is the argument from dreaming: we have all been deceived by dreams, so how can we trust our waking senses? In this passage, Al-Razi presents the objection and then delivers a crisp refutation that isolates the true source of error. Understanding this response is crucial for defending the reliability of empirical knowledge in Islamic theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
First, read the skeptic's argument: the sleeper sees something with conviction, then wakes up to find it false. The skeptic asks why there might not be a third state where waking perceptions are also false.
Notice the skeptical strategy: they use an analogy between dreaming and waking.
2
Read Al-Razi's refutation
Now read Al-Razi's response. He distinguishes the waking state from sleep: the awake person is attentive to the rules of wakefulness and can differentiate real perceptions from imaginary ones, while the sleeper is heedless of sensation.
The key is the phrase 'غافل عن الإحساس' (heedless of sensation).
3
Identify the shift in error source
Notice that Al-Razi does not deny that mistakes occur. Instead, he locates the error not in the senses but in the mind's failure to distinguish reality from its likeness due to inattention.
This preserves the reliability of sense perception when properly attentive.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this refutation is effective: it accepts the phenomenon (dreaming) but denies that it undermines waking knowledge. The skeptic's argument collapses because it misattributes the error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها : أن النائم يرى فى النوم شيئاً ويجزم بثبوته ، ثم يتبين له في اليقظة أن ذلك الجزم كان باطلا ، وإذا جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يكون هنا حالة ثالثة يظهر لنا فيها كذب ما رأيناه في اليقظة .

أقول : الدائم يرى فى خياله مثل ما يرى المستيقظ، إلا أن المستيقظ لما كان وانها على أحكام اليقظة حكم بأن أحد مراتبه واقع حق والآخر غير واقع وغير حق، والنائم لما كان غافلا عن الإحساس حسب أن الواقع الذى يراه في خياله وهذا ليس بغلط حتى ، بل هو غلط للنفس من عدم بين الشيء وبين مثاله حال الذهولى عن الشيء .
The third [objection]: The sleeper sees something in sleep and judges it to be real, then in wakefulness it becomes clear to him that that judgment was false. And if this is possible, then why may it not be that there is a third state in which the falsehood of what we saw in wakefulness appears to us?

I say: The awake person sees in his imagination similar to what the awake sees, except that the awake, because he is attentive to the rules of wakefulness, judges that one of his perceptions is real and the other is not real and not true. The sleeper, because he is heedless of sensation, supposes that the reality which he sees in his imagination is [real]. This is not a sensory error; rather it is an error of the self due to its failure to distinguish between the thing and its likeness in a state of heedlessness of the thing.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents a third skeptical objection against the reliability of sense perception, followed by a refutation. The objection is based on the phenomenon of dreaming: a sleeping person sees something with conviction, then upon waking realizes it was false. The skeptic argues that just as we can be mistaken in sleep, we might be in a third state (e.g., an unreal permanent condition) where our waking perceptions also prove false. The author responds by distinguishing between sensory perception and judgment. The awake person uses both imagination and sensation but applies the rules of wakefulness to differentiate real perceptions from imaginary ones. The dreamer is heedless of sensation and mistakenly takes imaginary images for reality. Thus the error is not in the senses but in the mind's failure to distinguish the object from its likeness due to inattention. Therefore, this does not impugn the reliability of sense perception itself.

Key Terms
النائم
اليقظة
الخيال
الإحساس
الغلط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dreams prove that sense perception is always unreliable.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that dreams only reveal error when one is heedless of sensation; waking senses, when properly attentive, remain reliable.

Checkpoint
Where does the error lie according to Al-Razi's response to the dreamer objection?
A) In the sense organ itself.
B) In the sleeper's heedlessness of sensation and subsequent misjudgment.
C) In the external object being non-existent.
D) In the faculty of imagination being defective.

Key Takeaway
Sensory error is not in the senses but in the mind's failure to distinguish reality from imagination due to heedlessness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you are 'asleep'—heedless of the truth—mistaking your own assumptions for reality? What would it take for you to wake up?
?
When you have been wrong about something in the past, was it due to a failure of evidence or a failure of your attention? How does Al-Razi's point affect your trust in your own senses?
?
