Refutation of the Delirious Patient Argument
Al-Razi refutes the skeptic's objection from the case of a delirious patient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi refutes the skeptical argument from the case of a delirious patient by appealing to the authority of pure reason rather than exhaustive proof.

Core Idea
Skepticism about the senses is defeated not by proving all error impossible, but by the immediate judgment of rational intuition.

This passage tackles one of the most powerful objections against sensory knowledge: the case of a delirious patient who vividly perceives nonexistent objects. The skeptic argues that if the sick can be mistaken, perhaps the healthy can too. Al-Razi engages with this objection through a dialectical exchange and then delivers his final refutation, grounding certainty in the innate verdict of reason rather than a chain of proofs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the skeptical objection
Read the first part of the passage (the objection) and the initial dialectical exchange. Notice how the skeptic uses the example of the delirious patient to challenge the reliability of sense perception in general.
The objection is presented as 'ورابعها'.
2
Examine the initial reply and its weakness
The objector suggests that illness is the cause, and in health it is absent. The initial response shows that removing one cause does not eliminate the possibility unless all causes are enumerated and excluded, which requires reasoning that itself depends on the senses. This leads to a regress.
The reply is introduced with 'فإن قلت' and 'قلت'.
3
Grasp al-Razi's final response
Al-Razi rejects the analogy between healthy and sick/sleeping perception on the basis that pure reason (al-ʿaql al-ṣarīḥ) repels error in healthy perception. He asserts that trust in the senses is grounded in rational intuition, not proof, so the skeptic's demand for exhaustive proof is irrelevant.
The final response begins with 'أقول'.
4
Reflect on the epistemological significance
Consider why al-Razi's move is philosophically significant: it avoids infinite regress by positing a foundational judgment of reason. The lesson is that some certainties are immediate and not subject to skeptical doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : أن صاحب البرسام قد يتصور صورا لا وجود لها فى الخارج، ويشاهدها ويجزم بثبوتها ووجودها ويصيح خوفا منها، وهذا يدل على أنه يجوز أن تعرض الإنسان حالة لاجلها يرى ما ليس بموجود في الخارج موجودا (۳) وإذا جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك فيما يشاهده الأصحاء.
فإن قلت : الموجب لتلك الحالة هو المرض فعند الصحة لا يوجد ·
قلت : انتفاء السبب الواحد لا يوجب انتفاء الحكم بل هذا الاحتمال لا يندفع إلا بحصر أسباب ذلك النخيل الكاذب ثم بيان انتفائها ، ثم إن المسبب لا يجوز حصوله ولا بقاؤه عند انتفاء الأسباب لكن كل واحدة من هذه المقدمات مما لا يمكن إثباته إلا بالنظر الدقيق لو أمكن، فيلزم أن لا يجوز الجزم بوجود شيء في المحسوسات إلا بعد العلم بتلك الأدلة وذلك مما يدل على أن مجرد حكم الحس غير مقبول .

أقول : لم يثبت الإحساس بشيء غير واقع في موضع أصلا وأما تجويز الغلط فيما يشاهده الأصحاء لتجويزه فيما يدركه النائم والمريض ما يأباه العقل الصريح ، ونحن لم نثبت الوثوق بالمحسوسات بدليل بل نقول المقل الصريح يقتضيه ، وهذه الأجوبة إنما نوردها لبيان أسباب الغلط الذهني بعد أن حكم العقل ذلك غلطا للذهن لا لإثبات صحة ما يدركه بالحواس كما قدمنا بيانه ، وأما قوله انتفاء السبب الواحد لا يوجب انتفاء الحكم قلنا : نعم لو أبقينا صحة الحسكم بثبوت المحسوسات في الخارج بدليل لكان الأمر على ما ذكره ، لكنا لم نثبت ذلك إلا بشهادة العقل من غير رجوعه إلى دليل ، فليس علينا أن نجيب عن هذه الإشكالات فإن احتمال الصحة هدم فيما يشاهده الأصحاء مندفع عند بديهة العقل من غير تأمل في الأسباب وحصرها وانتفائها ، وبيان امتناع حصول المسبب عند انتفاء الأسباب وغير ذلك مما يثبت بالنظر الدقيق والجليل .
The fourth [objection]: The person with delirium (ṣāḥib al-birsām) may conceive forms that have no existence externally, and he witnesses them and judges them to be real and existent, and screams in fear of them. This indicates that a person may undergo a state in which he sees what does not exist externally as existing. And if that is possible, why may it not be the case with what healthy people perceive?

If you say: The cause of that state is illness; in health it does not occur.
I say: The absence of one cause does not necessitate the absence of the judgment. Rather this possibility is not removed except by enumerating the causes of that false imagination and then showing their absence. Furthermore, the effect cannot occur or persist when the causes are absent. But each of these premises cannot be proven except by precise reasoning, if possible. So it follows that one cannot judge with certainty the existence of anything in the sensible things except after knowing those proofs. This indicates that mere sensory judgment is not acceptable.

I say: Sensation has not been established to perceive anything unreal in any instance. As for allowing error in what healthy people perceive because it is allowed in what the sleeper and the sick person perceive—this is rejected by pure reason. We did not establish trust in sensible things by a proof; rather we say that pure reason necessitates it. These answers are only presented to explain the causes of mental error after reason has judged them to be errors of the mind, not to prove the correctness of what is perceived by the senses, as we have previously explained. As for his statement that the absence of one cause does not necessitate the absence of the judgment, we say: Yes, if we had maintained the validity of sensory judgment of the existence of sense objects externally by a proof, then the matter would be as he said. But we only established that by the testimony of reason without recourse to a proof. So we are not obligated to respond to these objections. Indeed, the possibility of error in what healthy people see is repelled by the innate intuition of reason without reflection on the causes, their enumeration, their absence, demonstrating the impossibility of the effect when causes are absent, and other things that are established by precise and sublime reflection.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth skeptical objection, a dialectical exchange, and a comprehensive refutation. The objection is based on the case of a delirious patient who vividly perceives non-existent objects and reacts with fear. The skeptic argues that if such false perception is possible in illness, it might also occur in healthy people. The objector suggests that the cause is illness and hence absent in health. The initial response points out that removing one cause does not rule out other causes; to eliminate the possibility one must enumerate all causes of false imagination and prove their absence. This would require precise reasoning, which itself undermines immediate trust in sense. The author then gives his own response: sensation has never been proven to perceive something unreal; pure reason rejects granting error in healthy perception by analogy with sleepers and the sick. He asserts that trust in sensible things is not based on a proof but is necessitated by pure reason itself. Therefore, the skeptical objections are not binding; the possibility of error in healthy perception is repelled by the intuition of reason without needing to enumerate causes. This defense shows that the foundation of sensory knowledge is rational intuition, not inferential proof.

Key Terms
البرسام
النخيل الكاذب
المحسوسات
المقل الصريح
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That sensory skepticism must be defeated by exhaustively proving that all possible sources of error are absent in healthy perception.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that such exhaustive proof is unnecessary because pure reason (al-ʿaql al-ṣarīḥ) directly repels the possibility of error in healthy perception through innate intuition.

Checkpoint
According to the final response, how is trust in sense perception established?
A) By enumerating all possible causes of error.
B) By pure reason (al-ʿaql al-ṣarīḥ) without recourse to proof.
C) By divine revelation.
D) By consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
Pure reason (al-ʿaql al-ṣarīḥ) provides immediate certainty regarding sensory knowledge, independent of argumentative proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely been deceived by a mirage or a dream at some point. This lesson says that pure reason immediately rejects the idea that such errors make all your waking perceptions doubtful. Do you live with that kind of trust in your senses, or do you secretly harbor doubts that paralyze your daily decisions? What would change if you fully embraced that your reason already vouches for your senses?
?
Al-Razi says trust in the senses comes from the testimony of reason, not from a proof. In your own life, do you demand a proof for everything before you act, or do you sometimes rely on an immediate intuition that you know is true? Which area of your life—your faith, your relationships, your work—is most affected by your refusal to trust that intuition?
?
When you encounter an argument that claims to undermine something you hold as certain, like the existence of the external world, do you feel compelled to answer every objection? This lesson shows that al-Razi does not feel obligated to refute every skeptical puzzle because reason already settles the matter. Are there truths you have been trying to prove that you already know in your gut? Will you stop seeking proofs and start acting on what your reason already tells you?

The Skeptical Objection: Seeing Whiteness Without Color
Al-Razi enumerates examples of optical illusions to question sensory reliability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the skeptical argument that sensory perception can be deceptive, as illustrated by snow, crushed glass, and a glass crack appearing white while being colorless.

Core Idea
We see whiteness where there is no color—so can we trust our senses?

Al-Razi presents the fifth objection against the reliability of the senses. The objection uses everyday examples—snow, crushed glass, and a crack in thick glass—to show that we perceive colors that do not exist in the individual parts. This challenges the claim that sensory knowledge is certain. Let's read the objection carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage describing snow, crushed glass, and the glass crack. Notice how each example demonstrates whiteness without intrinsic color.
The examples are structured to build the skeptical conclusion.
2
Identify the argument structure
The objection claims: (1) We see whiteness. (2) The parts are colorless. (3) Therefore, sensory perception does not correspond to reality.
3
Consider the skeptic's point
The skeptic uses this to argue that we cannot rely on the senses to know the true nature of things.
This is a common skeptical challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسا : أنا ترى الثلج في غاية البياض ، ثم إذا ما لغتا في النظر إليه رأيناه مركبا من أجزاء جمدية صفار وكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء شفاف خال عن اللون ، فالثلج فى نفسه غير ملون ، مع أنا نراه ملونا بلون البياض. وليس لأحد أن يقول إن ذلك إنما كان لانعكاس الشعاع عن سطوح بعض تلك الأجزاء الجدية إلى بعض . لانا نقول : هذا لا يقدم في غرضنا ، لأن الذي ذكرته ليس إلا بان الملة التي لاجلها فى الثلج أبيض ، مع أنه فى نفسه ليس بأبيض ، ونحن ما سعينا إلا لهذا القدر ، وأيضاً فالرجاج المدقوق نراه أبيض مع أن كل من أجزائه شفاف خال عن اللون ولم يحدث فيما بينها كيفية مزاجية، لأن تلك الأجزاء صلبة يابسة لم يحصل فيما بينها فعل وانفعال، وأيضا نرى موضع الشق من الزجاج الثخين الشفاف أبيض مع أنه ليس هناك إلا الهواء المحتبس في ذلك الشق ، والهواء غير ملون والزجاج غير ملون فعلمنا أنا نرى الهواء مع أنه في نفسه واحد غير ملون ملونا .

أقول : قد تبين عند المحققين أن البياض إنما يتكون بتعاكس الضوءين من مسطوح أجسام مشفة والحمد والزجاج مشفان ، ولإشفافهما كان لهما ضوء، ومتى كانا ذوى سطح واحد لم يكن تعاكس ضوء منهما أما إذا انكسرا وحدث لهما سطوح تعاكس الضوء من بعضها إلى بعض فحدث البياض فإن لم يكن معها ما يوجب التزاق بعضها ببعض رأى كل واحد من أجزائها شفافا خاليا من اللون لعدم السطوح المختلفة في ذلك الواحد الذي هو شرط في حديث البياض ، وإذا عرض معها ما يوجب التزاق بعضها ببعض صار جسما واحدا أبيض كما فى بياض البيض المسلوق ، فإنه قبل السلق كا، له ضوء ولم يكن فيه قابل ضوء كما في الماء ، وبعد السلق يعاكس الضوء بين ذى الضوء وبين قابله فحدث البياض والماء إذا كان مائعا ذا سطح واحد كان له ضوء ولم يمكن فيه قابل ضوء فلم يكن فيه تعاكس، أما إذا تريد أو الجمد اجتمع الأمر بأن فيه وحدث البياض وفى بياض البيض المسلوق ما يوجب فيه مع ذلك الالتزاق والتماسك فصار جسما واحدا أبيض ولم يكن امتياز بعض أجزائه من ، فلا يتبين للمتأمل فيه شف الجزء الواحد كما في الثلج والزجاج ، فظهر من ذلك أن مانراه ملونا فهو في نفسه غير ملون ، لأن اللون ليس إلا الغرض الموصوف بتلك الصفة، ولم يجب من ذلك أن كل مالا يكون جزوه ملونا يمتنع أن تكون أجزاؤه ملونات .
And the fifth [objection]: We see snow extremely white, then if we examine it closely we see it composed of small icy particles, each of which is transparent and colorless. So snow in itself is non-colored, although we see it colored with the color of whiteness. And no one may say that this is only due to the reflection of light from the surfaces of some of those icy particles onto others. For we say: This does not serve our purpose, because what you have mentioned is only an explanation of the reason why snow appears white, while it is not in itself white. We only seek this much. Also, crushed glass (zujāj) we see white, although each of its particles is transparent and colorless, and no mixed quality occurred between them, because those particles are hard and dry, with no action and reaction between them. Also, we see the place of a crack in thick transparent glass as white, although there is nothing there but air trapped in the crack. Air is non-colored and glass is non-colored. So we know that we see air, while it is in itself one and non-colored, as colored.

I say: It has been established among the investigators that whiteness is generated only by the mutual reflection of two lights from the surfaces of transparent bodies. Snow and glass are transparent, and due to their transparency they have light. When they have a single surface, there is no mutual reflection of light from them. But if they are broken and have surfaces, light reflects from some to others, so whiteness occurs. If nothing causes their parts to adhere to each other, each of their parts is seen as transparent and colorless due to the lack of different surfaces in that one [part] which is a condition for the occurrence of whiteness. If something occurs that causes their parts to adhere, they become a single white body, like the whiteness of boiled egg white. Before boiling, it had light but no recipient of light, as in water. After boiling, light reflects between the one having light and its recipient, so whiteness occurs. Water, when liquid with a single surface, has light but cannot have a recipient of light, so there is no reflection. But if it freezes (tajammad), both matters combine and whiteness occurs. In boiled egg white, there is adhesion and cohesion, so it becomes a single white body, and the transparency of the single part is not apparent to the observer as in snow and glass. So it appears from this that what we see as colored is in itself non-colored, because color is nothing but the accident described by that quality. And it does not follow from this that everything whose parts are not colored precludes that its parts could be colored.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth skeptical objection using visual examples: snow appears white, but close inspection shows it consists of transparent ice crystals; crushed glass appears white, yet each shard is transparent; the crack in thick glass appears white, although only air is there. The skeptic argues that since we see colors where there are none, sensory perception can be deceptive. The author provides an explanation: whiteness results from mutual reflection of light off multiple surfaces of transparent bodies. When snow falls as small crystals with many facets, light reflects between them and creates the appearance of whiteness, even though each crystal is colorless. Similarly, crushed glass has many surfaces; the crack in glass creates such surfaces. This optical phenomenon does not mean the senses lie; rather it shows that we perceive an effect of real physical interaction. The author clarifies that color is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that inheres in a body; the appearance of whiteness is a real accident produced by the arrangement, even though the individual parts lack color. Therefore, the senses are not mistaken—they correctly perceive the whiteness that actually exists as an accident of the aggregate. This defense upholds the reliability of sensory perception while explaining how such appearances arise from natural causes.

Key Terms
الثلج
البياض
الشفاف
الزجاج
الهواء
اللون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We see white, so snow must be white all the way down.
Correction: The objection shows that whiteness can appear without color in the parts, challenging naive realism.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the examples used in the skeptical objection?
A) Snow
B) Crushed glass
C) A crack in thick glass
D) Boiled egg white

Key Takeaway
The skeptical objection uses optical illusions to argue that our senses can show colors where none exist in the parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been absolutely certain of something you saw, only to later discover it was not as it seemed? How does this affect your trust in your own judgment? What does this teach you about the need for deeper investigation before forming beliefs?
?
If your senses can deceive you about something as simple as color, what else might they deceive you about? How can you guard against being misled by your own perceptions in matters of faith?
?
Al-Razi uses these examples to question sensory reliability. How does this challenge affect your confidence in your knowledge of God gained through observation of the world? What role should reason play in confirming what the senses report?

al-Razi's Response: Whiteness from Mutual Light Reflection
Al-Razi provides a physical and metaphysical explanation of whiteness as an accident of light reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's explanation that apparent whiteness in transparent objects is a real accident caused by mutual reflection of light, and that this does not imply sensory deception.

Core Idea
Whiteness is a real accident generated by light reflecting between surfaces, not an illusion.

After presenting the skeptical challenge, al-Razi provides a detailed scientific and philosophical response. He explains that whiteness is not a property of the individual parts but emerges from the interaction of light with multiple surfaces. This upholds the reliability of the senses, as they accurately perceive a real accident. Let's examine his explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation
Read al-Razi's response starting with 'I say'. Note how he defines whiteness as resulting from mutual reflection of light from surfaces of transparent bodies.
2
Understand the condition for whiteness
Whiteness requires multiple surfaces to reflect light. Single-surface transparent bodies do not show whiteness.
3
Apply the explanation to the examples
See how snow, crushed glass, and cracks meet the condition: they have many small surfaces. Boiled egg white is an example where parts adhere, appearing uniformly white.
4
Draw the philosophical conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that color is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that can exist in the whole without existing in each part. The senses are not deceived.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسا : أنا ترى الثلج في غاية البياض ، ثم إذا ما لغتا في النظر إليه رأيناه مركبا من أجزاء جمدية صفار وكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء شفاف خال عن اللون ، فالثلج فى نفسه غير ملون ، مع أنا نراه ملونا بلون البياض. وليس لأحد أن يقول إن ذلك إنما كان لانعكاس الشعاع عن سطوح بعض تلك الأجزاء الجدية إلى بعض . لانا نقول : هذا لا يقدم في غرضنا ، لأن الذي ذكرته ليس إلا بان الملة التي لاجلها فى الثلج أبيض ، مع أنه فى نفسه ليس بأبيض ، ونحن ما سعينا إلا لهذا القدر ، وأيضاً فالرجاج المدقوق نراه أبيض مع أن كل من أجزائه شفاف خال عن اللون ولم يحدث فيما بينها كيفية مزاجية، لأن تلك الأجزاء صلبة يابسة لم يحصل فيما بينها فعل وانفعال، وأيضا نرى موضع الشق من الزجاج الثخين الشفاف أبيض مع أنه ليس هناك إلا الهواء المحتبس في ذلك الشق ، والهواء غير ملون والزجاج غير ملون فعلمنا أنا نرى الهواء مع أنه في نفسه واحد غير ملون ملونا .

أقول : قد تبين عند المحققين أن البياض إنما يتكون بتعاكس الضوءين من مسطوح أجسام مشفة والحمد والزجاج مشفان ، ولإشفافهما كان لهما ضوء، ومتى كانا ذوى سطح واحد لم يكن تعاكس ضوء منهما أما إذا انكسرا وحدث لهما سطوح تعاكس الضوء من بعضها إلى بعض فحدث البياض فإن لم يكن معها ما يوجب التزاق بعضها ببعض رأى كل واحد من أجزائها شفافا خاليا من اللون لعدم السطوح المختلفة في ذلك الواحد الذي هو شرط في حديث البياض ، وإذا عرض معها ما يوجب التزاق بعضها ببعض صار جسما واحدا أبيض كما فى بياض البيض المسلوق ، فإنه قبل السلق كا، له ضوء ولم يكن فيه قابل ضوء كما في الماء ، وبعد السلق يعاكس الضوء بين ذى الضوء وبين قابله فحدث البياض والماء إذا كان مائعا ذا سطح واحد كان له ضوء ولم يمكن فيه قابل ضوء فلم يكن فيه تعاكس، أما إذا تريد أو الجمد اجتمع الأمر بأن فيه وحدث البياض وفى بياض البيض المسلوق ما يوجب فيه مع ذلك الالتزاق والتماسك فصار جسما واحدا أبيض ولم يكن امتياز بعض أجزائه من ، فلا يتبين للمتأمل فيه شف الجزء الواحد كما في الثلج والزجاج ، فظهر من ذلك أن مانراه ملونا فهو في نفسه غير ملون ، لأن اللون ليس إلا الغرض الموصوف بتلك الصفة، ولم يجب من ذلك أن كل مالا يكون جزوه ملونا يمتنع أن تكون أجزاؤه ملونات .

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a white object, do you take it for granted that whiteness is simply 'there'? How does this lesson challenge you to think about the hidden conditions that produce what you perceive? Are you willing to admit that your immediate experience is only the surface of a deeper reality?
?
If color is an accident of aggregation, what does that mean for how you trust your senses? Do you habitually assume that the world is exactly as it appears? This lesson invites you to examine whether you are too quick to judge the simple as simple—where else might you be oversimplifying?
?
Consider an area of your life where you see only the composite picture (e.g., your community, your own character) without investigating the parts. How might the truth be more nuanced? What would it cost you to look at the underlying 'transparent parts' rather than the surface 'whiteness'? Are you prepared to let go of comfortable certainties for a more complex truth?

Sense Perception Cannot Be the Primary Judge
Imam Razi proves sense perception cannot be trusted because it can err.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that sense perception requires a higher judge to distinguish truth from error, and why it cannot be the ultimate foundation of knowledge.

Core Idea
Sense perception can be wrong, so it needs a higher judge.

Imam Razi presents a concise but powerful argument against relying on sense perception as the ultimate arbiter of truth. He uses the analogy of an accused witness to show that since senses can err, we cannot trust them without a higher authority. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the translation. Notice the key terms: حكم الحس, حاكم آخر.
The passage has two parts: the main argument and a marginal note.
2
Understand the core argument
The argument is: sense judgments can be false or true. Since they can be false, we cannot rely on them (like an accused witness). Therefore, we need a higher judge to correct them.
3
Analyze the metaphor
Why the analogy of an accused person? In Islamic law, the testimony of an accused person is not accepted because they have a motivation to lie. Similarly, sense perception is 'accused' because it can err.
4
Consider the marginal note
The marginal note (أقول) makes a stronger claim: sense has no judgment at all. This reflects a more radical skeptical position.
This note might be from a commentator or the author himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت بهذه الوجوه أن حكم الحس قد يكون باطلا وقد يكون حقا وإذا كان كذلك لم يجز الاعتماد على حكمه إذ لا شهادة المتهم بل لابد من حاكم آخر فوقه ليميز خطاه عن صوابه، على هذا التقدير لا يكون الحس هو الحاكم الأول وهو المطلوب.
أقول: قد ظهر أن الحس ليس له حكم في شيء من المواضع، فبطل القول بأن حكم الحس قد يكون باطلا ولذلك كان غير معتمد عليه.
So it is established by these aspects that the judgment of sense may be false and may be true. Since this is the case, it is not permissible to rely on its judgment, because the testimony of an accused person is not accepted. Rather, there must be another judge above it to distinguish its error from its correctness. On this basis, sense is not the primary judge, which is what was desired.
I say: It has become apparent that sense has no judgment in any matter, so the statement that the judgment of sense may be false is invalid, and therefore it is not relied upon.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against the reliability of sense perception. The argument proceeds: sense judgments can be either true or false. Because they can be false, we cannot rely on them—just as we do not accept the testimony of an accused person. Instead, we need a higher judge (intellect or some other faculty) to discriminate between sense's errors and correct judgments. Thus sense cannot be the primary source of knowledge. The marginal note (أقول) adds an even stronger claim: sense has no judgment at all, so the very idea that it can be false is invalid, meaning sense is simply not a source of knowledge. The note seems to reject the premise that sense can be true, emphasizing that sense does not judge anything objectively. This shows the author's critical stance on empiricism.

Key Terms
حكم الحس
الاعتماد على الحس
حاكم آخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the proof argues that sense perception is completely useless and should be abandoned.
Correction: The proof only argues that sense perception cannot be the final arbiter of truth; it still provides data that must be judged by a higher faculty like reason.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that sense perception cannot be relied upon as a primary source of knowledge?
A) Because sense judgments are always false.
B) Because sense judgments can be either true or false, and a fallible witness is unreliable.
C) Because sense perception is subjective and varies from person to person.
D) Because sense perception contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Sense perception is fallible and thus cannot be the ultimate judge of truth; a higher faculty (reason) is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision based solely on what you see or feel, have you ever been deceived? How does this lesson challenge your reliance on your own perceptions? Will you now seek a higher judge (intellect or revelation) before trusting your senses? Commit to one situation this week where you will deliberately question your sense impressions.
?
The passage compares sense to an accused person whose testimony is not accepted. In your daily life, do you routinely accept your own perceptions as true without scrutiny? How often do you subject your sensory experiences to critical examination? What step will you take today to cultivate that habit?
?
If sense is not the primary judge, what is? The lesson implies a higher judge exists. Have you consciously submitted your judgments to that higher judge (your intellect guided by revelation)? If not, what is holding you back?

Sense Cannot Give Universals
Imam Razi argues that sense cannot give universals and addresses a potential contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sense perception alone cannot yield universal concepts and how the author resolves an apparent inconsistency in his own classification.

Core Idea
Sense gives particulars, not universals.

Imam Razi presents a second argument for the necessity of rational judgment: sense perception only grasps individual instances. Even after perceiving many cases, the universal judgment that 'every whole is greater than its part' cannot be sensed because it covers all possible cases, including those never experienced. This lesson unpacks that argument and the author's self-critical note, which questions his earlier classification of 'the sun is luminous' as a sensed judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the flow of the argument. Notice the two parts: the main argument and the marginal note.
2
Identify the key claim
Identify that sense only perceives this whole and this part, not the universal 'every whole is greater than its part'. Understand why universals cannot come from sense even after many observations.
The universal applies to all possible instances, not just those perceived.
3
Examine the self-critique
Notice that the author points out a tension: earlier he listed 'the sun is luminous' as a sensed judgment, but now he says sense cannot give universals. See how he resolves it by saying such judgments have sensible principles but are rational.
This shows the author's intellectual honesty.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is foundational for kalam epistemology: it establishes that universal concepts and necessary judgments require a rational source beyond sense.
This connects to the broader theme of rational judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكليات فالحس لا يعطيها البتة، فإن الحس لا يشاهد إلا هذا الكل وهذا الجزء، فأما وصف الأعظمية فهو غير مدرك بالحس، ويقدر أن يكون ذلك الوصف مدركا لكن المدرك هو أن هذا الكل أعظم من هذا الجزء، فأما أن كل كل فهو أعظم من جزئه فغير مدرك بالحس، ولو أدرك كل ما في الوجود من الكليات والأجزاء، لأن قولنا كل كذا ليس المراد منه كل ما في الوجود الخارجي من تلك الماهية فقط، بل كل ما لو وجد في الخارج أصدق عليه أنه فرد من أفراد تلك الماهية، وذلك مما لا يمكن وقوع الإحساس به، فثبت أن الحس لا قدرة له على إعطاء الكليات البتة.
أقول: قد عد في الحسيات في صدر الباب العلم بأن الشمس مضيئة والنار حارة من غير تقييدهما بما يحمل الحكم شخصيا، وحكم ههنا بأن الحس لا يقوى على إعطاء الكليات البتة وذلك يقتضى أن لا يكون ما عده في الحسيات حسيا، بل مبدأه يكون حسيا، وقد قال ههنا أن الحس لا يشاهد إلا هذا الكل وهذا الجزء، فإن لزمه أن يكون الحكم بكون النار حارة وكون الكل أعظم من الجزء متساويا في كونهما عقليين ولهما مبادئ محسوسة وهذا خيط ظاهر.
As for universals, sense does not give them at all, for sense does not perceive except this whole and this part. As for the attribute of greatness, it is not perceived by sense. It may be that that attribute is perceived, but what is perceived is that this whole is greater than this part. But that every whole is greater than its part is not perceived by sense. Even if it perceived everything in existence of universals and parts, because our saying 'every such-and-such' does not mean everything in external existence of that essence only, but rather everything that if it existed externally, it would be true to say that it is an individual of that essence, and that is something that cannot be sensed. So it is established that sense has no power to give universals at all.
I say: [The author] counted among the sensed things at the beginning of the chapter the knowledge that the sun is luminous and fire is hot without restricting them to what makes the judgment personal, and here he rules that sense cannot give universals at all, and that requires that what he counted among the sensed things is not sensed, but rather its principle is sensed. And he said here that sense does not perceive except this whole and this part, then it would follow that the judgment that fire is hot and that the whole is greater than the part are equal in being rational and having sensible principles, and this is an apparent thread.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage provides a second argument: sense perception can only grasp particular instances (this whole, this part), not universal statements like 'every whole is greater than its part.' Even if we perceive many wholes and parts, the universal judgment involves an 'every' that applies to all possible cases, including those never perceived. This goes beyond the capacity of sense. The marginal note raises a self-critique: earlier in the chapter, the author listed knowledge that 'the sun is luminous' as a sensed thing, but now he denies sense can give universals. The note suggests that such 'sensed' judgments are actually rational judgments with sensible origins, not purely sensed. The phrase 'this is an apparent thread' likely means the connection is weak or contradictory.

Key Terms
الكليات
الحس
الأعظمية
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sense can provide universals by repeated observation.
Correction: The passage argues that even after perceiving many wholes, the universal 'every whole is greater than its part' refers to all possible wholes, including those never perceived. This necessity and generality cannot come from sense.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that sense cannot give universal judgments like 'every whole is greater than its part'?
A) Because sense can only perceive particular instances, not all possible cases.
B) Because sense is too weak to perceive large objects.
C) Because universals are innate and do not come from sense.
D) Because sense only gives falsehoods.

Key Takeaway
Sense perception alone cannot yield universal concepts; they require rational inference that goes beyond particular instances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged a person's character based on a single encounter, assuming that one instance defines them entirely? This lesson shows sense gives only particulars, not universals. How can you guard against making universal judgments from limited sense experiences?
?

?
The argument concludes that sense cannot give universals. If your belief in Allah's attributes (e.g., His power, wisdom) were based only on what you see in creation, would that belief be rational? How does this lesson challenge you to deepen your reliance on reason and revelation rather than mere sense?

The Third Group's Core Argument
The group states its position and root-branch analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third group's skeptical position that challenges self-evident truths by arguing that all intelligibles are derived from sensibles, making sense knowledge more fundamental.

Core Idea
Sensation is the root, intellect is the branch—so sense is stronger.

This group accepts sense perceptions but rejects the certainty of self-evident truths. They argue that every rational concept originates from sensory experience, so the source (sense) must be more reliable than its derivative (intellect). This lesson introduces their core claim and sets the stage for their detailed proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the group's stance. Then read the translation.
Notice how they frame intelligibles as branches of sensibles.
2
Identify the group's stance
They accept sense perceptions but attack self-evident truths. Understand this distinction.
3
Analyze the root-branch analogy
They say intelligibles are branches of sensibles, so the root (sense) is stronger than the branch (intellect). Why would that follow?
They also give examples: blind and impotent lose knowledge due to missing senses.
4
Reflect on the implications
If sense is stronger, then self-evident truths derived from sense might be weaker. This is the foundation for their five proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الثالثة: الذين يعترفون بالحسيات ويقدحون في البديهيات قالوا: المعقولات فرع المحسوسات، ولذلك فإن من فقد حاسة فقد علما، كالأكمه والعنين، والأصل أقوى من الفرع.
ثم الذي يدل على ضعف البديهيات وجوه خمسة.
أحدها: أن أجل البديهيات العلم بأن الشيء إما أن يكون وإما أن لا يكون، ثم إن هذه القضية ليست يقينية، وإذا لم يكن أقوى الأوليات يقينيا فما ظنك بأضعفها. بيان الأول وهو أنا رأينا المعولين على البديهيات يذكرون لها أمثلة أربعة:
أحدها: أن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان.
وثانيها: أن الكل أعظم من الجزء.
وثالثها: أن الأشياء المساوية للشيء الواحد متساوية.
ورابعها: أن الجسم الواحد لا يكون في مكانين.
ووجدنا هذه الثلاثة الأخيرة متفرعة على الأول.
أقول: إذا كان الإحساس شرطا في حصول حكم عقلي لم يجب من ذلك أن يكون الإحساس أقوى من التعقل، فإن الاستعداد شرط في حصول الكمال وليس بأقوى من الكمال.
أقول: أو كانت الثلاثة الأخيرة متفرعة على الأول لكانت نظرية غير بديهية لكنهم عدوهاني في البديهيات، فعلمنا أن اعتمادهم في الحكم بصحتها على بديهة العقل لا على مقدمة أخرى.
The third group: those who acknowledge sense perceptions and criticize self-evident truths. They said: Intelligibles are branches of sensibles, therefore whoever loses a sense loses knowledge, like the blind and the impotent, and the root is stronger than the branch.
Then what indicates the weakness of self-evident truths are five aspects.
One: The clearest of self-evident truths is the knowledge that a thing either exists or does not exist, yet this proposition is not certain. If the strongest of primary truths is not certain, what do you think of the weaker ones? The explanation of the first is that we see those who rely on self-evident truths mention for them four examples:
One: Negation and affirmation do not coexist and do not cease.
Second: The whole is greater than the part.
Third: Things equal to the same thing are equal.
Fourth: One body cannot be in two places.
And we find that these last three are derived from the first.
I say: If sensation is a condition for obtaining a rational judgment, it does not necessarily follow that sensation is stronger than reasoning, because readiness is a condition for obtaining perfection and is not stronger than perfection.
I say: If the last three were derived from the first, they would be theoretical, not self-evident, but they count them among the self-evident truths, so we know that their reliance in judging their correctness is on the intuition of the intellect, not on another premise.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage introduces a skeptical group that accepts sense perceptions but attacks self-evident truths (badihiyat). They argue that intelligibles are derived from sensibles (so sense is more fundamental). Then they give five proofs for the weakness of self-evident truths. Only the first proof is detailed here: the clearest self-evident truth—the law of non-contradiction—is not certain. To show this, they note that the four standard examples of self-evident truths include the law of non-contradiction (negation and affirmation do not coexist) and three others (whole-part, equality, one body in two places). They claim the last three depend on the first, so if the first is uncertain, they are even weaker. The marginal notes respond: the first note argues that sensation being a condition does not make it stronger; readiness for perfection is not stronger than perfection. The second note points out that if the three are derived, they would be theoretical, not self-evident, but proponents count them as self-evident, which suggests they rely on intellectual intuition, not deduction.

Key Terms
البديهيات
المعقولات
المحسوسات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The group completely denies self-evident truths.
Correction: They accept sense perceptions and only argue that self-evident truths are not certain.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the third group use to argue that sense is stronger than intellect?
A) The sun is brighter than the moon.
B) The root is stronger than the branch.
C) The whole is greater than the part.
D) Things equal to the same thing are equal.

Key Takeaway
The third group's core argument is that since all intelligibles derive from sensibles, sensation is a more reliable source of knowledge than rational intuition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on your senses, do you ever stop to wonder if they are deceiving you? How does the certainty of your faith compare to the certainty of what you see with your eyes?
?
This group says loss of a sense means loss of knowledge. What kind of knowledge would you lose if you were cut off from your senses? Is your belief in the unseen any less real than your sight?

First Proof: The Law of Non-Contradiction is Uncertain
The group presents the first proof that the law of non-contradiction is uncertain, with four examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the skeptics' first proof that even the clearest self-evident truth, the law of non-contradiction, is not certain, and how it allegedly undermines all other self-evident truths.

Core Idea
If the strongest self-evident truth is uncertain, the rest are even more doubtful.

The group now elaborates the first of their five proofs. They target the most fundamental self-evident principle—the law of non-contradiction—and argue that it lacks certainty. They then show that the other standard examples of self-evident truths (whole-part, equality, one body in two places) are derived from this principle, so they are also uncertain. This lesson unpacks that argument and the four examples.

1
Read the passage

They present five proofs but only detail the first here.
3

Note: the first example is the law of non-contradiction itself.
4
Understand the derivation claim
They assert that the last three examples are derived from the first. Therefore, if the first is uncertain, the others are even weaker.
This is meant to show the weakness of all self-evident truths.

Imam Razi's Two Refutations
Two refutations: condition vs. cause, and derivation vs. self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two refutations offered by Imam Razi against the skeptics' argument, distinguishing between a condition and a cause, and showing that the claimed derivation would undermine self-evidence.

Core Idea
Sensation is a condition for intellect, not a cause, so intellect remains superior.

After presenting the skeptics' argument, the author (Imam Razi) provides two marginal notes to refute it. The first note argues that being a condition for something does not mean it is stronger; readiness for perfection is not stronger than perfection. The second note points out that if the three examples were truly derived from the first, they would be theoretical, not self-evident—yet proponents count them as self-evident, showing that the classification relies on intellectual intuition, not deduction. This lesson analyses these important responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the responses
Read the two 'أقول' sections in Arabic and their translations.
These are the author's own words, responding to the skeptics.
2
Understand the first response
The first response challenges the root-branch analogy: sensation is a condition (like readiness) for rational judgment, but a condition is not necessarily stronger than the perfection it enables.
Example: wood is a condition for fire, but wood is not stronger than fire.
3
Understand the second response
The second response points out a contradiction: if the last three examples are derived from the first, they would be theoretical, not self-evident. But proponents call them self-evident, so their reliance must be on intellectual intuition, not derivation.
This shows that the skeptics' own reasoning undermines their attack.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الثالثة: الذين يعترفون بالحسيات ويقدحون في البديهيات قالوا: المعقولات فرع المحسوسات، ولذلك فإن من فقد حاسة فقد علما، كالأكمه والعنين، والأصل أقوى من الفرع.
ثم الذي يدل على ضعف البديهيات وجوه خمسة.
أحدها: أن أجل البديهيات العلم بأن الشيء إما أن يكون وإما أن لا يكون، ثم إن هذه القضية ليست يقينية، وإذا لم يكن أقوى الأوليات يقينيا فما ظنك بأضعفها. بيان الأول وهو أنا رأينا المعولين على البديهيات يذكرون لها أمثلة أربعة:
أحدها: أن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان.
وثانيها: أن الكل أعظم من الجزء.
وثالثها: أن الأشياء المساوية للشيء الواحد متساوية.
ورابعها: أن الجسم الواحد لا يكون في مكانين.
ووجدنا هذه الثلاثة الأخيرة متفرعة على الأول.
أقول: إذا كان الإحساس شرطا في حصول حكم عقلي لم يجب من ذلك أن يكون الإحساس أقوى من التعقل، فإن الاستعداد شرط في حصول الكمال وليس بأقوى من الكمال.
أقول: أو كانت الثلاثة الأخيرة متفرعة على الأول لكانت نظرية غير بديهية لكنهم عدوهاني في البديهيات، فعلمنا أن اعتمادهم في الحكم بصحتها على بديهة العقل لا على مقدمة أخرى.
The third group: those who acknowledge sense perceptions and criticize self-evident truths. They said: Intelligibles are branches of sensibles, therefore whoever loses a sense loses knowledge, like the blind and the impotent, and the root is stronger than the branch.
Then what indicates the weakness of self-evident truths are five aspects.
One: The clearest of self-evident truths is the knowledge that a thing either exists or does not exist, yet this proposition is not certain. If the strongest of primary truths is not certain, what do you think of the weaker ones? The explanation of the first is that we see those who rely on self-evident truths mention for them four examples:
One: Negation and affirmation do not coexist and do not cease.
Second: The whole is greater than the part.
Third: Things equal to the same thing are equal.
Fourth: One body cannot be in two places.
And we find that these last three are derived from the first.
I say: If sensation is a condition for obtaining a rational judgment, it does not necessarily follow that sensation is stronger than reasoning, because readiness is a condition for obtaining perfection and is not stronger than perfection.
I say: If the last three were derived from the first, they would be theoretical, not self-evident, but they count them among the self-evident truths, so we know that their reliance in judging their correctness is on the intuition of the intellect, not on another premise.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage introduces a skeptical group that accepts sense perceptions but attacks self-evident truths (badihiyat). They argue that intelligibles are derived from sensibles (so sense is more fundamental). Then they give five proofs for the weakness of self-evident truths. Only the first proof is detailed here: the clearest self-evident truth—the law of non-contradiction—is not certain. To show this, they note that the four standard examples of self-evident truths include the law of non-contradiction (negation and affirmation do not coexist) and three others (whole-part, equality, one body in two places). They claim the last three depend on the first, so if the first is uncertain, they are even weaker. The marginal notes respond: the first note argues that sensation being a condition does not make it stronger; readiness for perfection is not stronger than perfection. The second note points out that if the three are derived, they would be theoretical, not self-evident, but proponents count them as self-evident, which suggests they rely on intellectual intuition, not deduction.

Key Terms
الاستعداد
الكمال
النظرية
بديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensation being a condition for intellect implies sensation is stronger.
Correction: A condition is not necessarily stronger; readiness for perfection is not stronger than perfection.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the first response use to show that a condition is not necessarily stronger?
A) Wood is a condition for fire but not stronger.
B) Water is a condition for fish but not stronger.
C) Readiness is a condition for perfection but not stronger.
D) Light is a condition for vision but not stronger.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the skeptics by clarifying that sensation is a condition for intellect, not a cause, and that the claimed derivation of self-evident truths conflicts with their classification as self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your faith have you allowed sensory experience to dominate over intellect, forgetting that sensation is merely a condition and not the cause or superior? What would it take for you to submit to the self-evident truths of the intellect that come directly from God?
?
Consider the self-evident truths of Islam—like the existence of God, the necessity of worship, the obligation of justice. Do you treat these as self-evident and act on them immediately, or do you hesitate, wanting more proof? What change is Allah calling you to make today?
?
The skeptics' error was confusing condition with cause. In your spiritual life, are there practices or experiences that you treat as causes of faith rather than mere conditions? How can you realign your understanding to give glory to Allah as the true cause, and not to the means?

Rational Judgments

The Three Self-Evident Truths and Their Proofs
Imam Razi proves three self-evident truths by reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi proves three self-evident truths using the principle of non-contradiction.

Core Idea
Self-evident truths are proven by showing that their denial leads to contradiction.

Imam Razi provides logical arguments for three propositions that appear self-evident: the whole is greater than the part, things equal to the same thing are equal, and one body cannot be in two places at once. Each proof works by showing that the opposite would result in a logical impossibility—something being both existent and non-existent simultaneously.

Guided Path
1
Read the three proofs carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that each proof ends with a contradiction.
2
Identify the structure of each proof
Each proof is a reductio ad absurdum. Find the condition that is denied and the resulting contradiction.
The contradiction is always that something would be both existent and non-existent.
3
Compare the three proofs
Notice how they all rely on the same principle: the impossibility of combining existence and non-existence.
4
Reflect on the necessity of proof
Ask yourself why these seemingly obvious truths need proof. This will be addressed in the next lesson.
Consider the objection that intuition suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قولنا: الكل أعظم من الجزء، لأنه لو لم يكن كذلك لكان وجود الجزء الآخر وعدمه بمثابة واحدة، لحينئذ يجتمع في ذلك الجزء الآخر كونه موجودا معدوما معا.
وأما قولنا: الأشياء المساوية للشيء الواحد متساوية، لأنه لو لم يكن كذلك لكان الألف المحكوم عليه بأنه يساوي السواد سوادا لا محالة، ومن حيث إنه محكوم عليه بأنه يساوي ما ليس بسواد يجب أن لا يكون سوادا، فلو كان الألف مساويا للأمرين لزم أن يكون الألف في نفسه سوادا وأن لا يكون في نفسه سوادا، فيجتمع النفي والإثبات.
وأما قولنا: إن الجسم الواحد في الآن الواحد لا يكون في مكانين، لأنه لو جاز ذلك لما تميز الجسم الواحد الحاصل في مكانين متبايزين عن الجسمين اللذين حصلا كذلك، وحينئذ لا يتميز وجود الجسم الآخر عن عدمه، فيصدق عليه كونه موجودا معدوما معا.
لا يقال: كل عاقل يعلم بالبديهة حقيقة هذه القضايا الثلاثة وإن لم يخطر بباله هذه الحجة التي ذكرتموها. لأنا نقول: لا نسلم أن حكم العقلاء بهذه القضايا غير متوقف على الحجة التي ذكرناها، ولذلك يقولون: لو لم يكن الكل أعظم من الجزء لم يكن للأجزاء الأخر أثر البتة، ولو كان الشيء الواحد مساويا لمختلفين لكان ذلك الواحد مخالفا لنفسه، وهذا إشارة إلى ما ذكرناه، نعم قد لا يمكنهم التعبير عن الحجة على الوجه الذي لخصناه، ولكن معناه مقرر في عقولهم ولا عبرة بالعبارة.
فقد لاح بأن أجل البديهيات قولنا النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان وهو غير يقيني.
أقول: هذا البيان مبني على كون الكل هو الجزء مع زيادة، ولا نعني بقولنا الكل أعظم من الجزء إلا هذا، فهو إن كان حجة على ثبوت هذا الحكم لكان مصادرة على المطلوب.
أقول: هذا بيان أن الشيء المساوي لمختلفين مخالف لنفسه، وهو عين ما ادعى بيانه، فإن أراد به البيان بالخلف فليس قولنا المساوي لمختلفين مخالف لنفسه بأوضح من قولنا المساويان لشيء بعينه متساويان حتى يتبين هذا بذاك.
أقول: عدم الامتياز لا يكون هو الاتحاد، فإن المثلين من كل جهة لا يتميزان ومع ذلك لا يكونان واحدا، وكان من الصواب أن يقول: لو كان جسم في مكانين لكان المكان الواحد اثنين، وحينئذ يكون وجود أحد المثلين وعدمه واحدا، مع أن الحكم المذكور غير محتاج إلى هذا البيان.
أقول: الكل هو جزءان، والجزء هو أحدهما، ولا يحتاج في أن الشيء مع غيره أكبر منه وحده إلى أن تعرف أن الأحد الجزأين أثر أولا، والحكم بأن كون الشيء مساويا لمختلفين مقتض لمخالفته لنفسه بيان لكون الشيئين المساويين لشيء ما متساويان ليس بأولى من أن يكون هذا الثاني بيانا للأول، فإن الحجة ينبغي أن تكون أبين من الدعوى، وليس ههنا لأحد الحكمين فضيلة في كونه أبين من الآخر، ودعوى أن كل من تصور هذه القضايا تصور هذه الحجج وإن لم يقدر على تلخيصه في العبارة غير مسلم.
أقول: لاشك في أنه أجل من غيره، ولذلك سماه الحكماء أول الأوائل، يعني في الوضوح، وكونه أوضح يدل على وضوح غيره ولا يدل على احتياج غيره في الوضوح إليه.
As for our statement: The whole is greater than the part, because if it were not so, the existence of the other part and its nonexistence would be equivalent, then that other part would simultaneously be existent and non-existent.
And as for our statement: Things equal to the same thing are equal, because if it were not so, then the Alpha which is judged to be equal to blackness is necessarily black, and from the perspective that it is judged to be equal to what is not black, it must not be black. So if the Alpha is equal to the two, it would follow that the Alpha in itself is black and not black, thus negation and affirmation coexist.
And as for our statement: One body cannot be in two places at one instant, because if that were permissible, then the single body existing in two distinct places would not be distinguished from two bodies that also exist thus, and then the existence of the other body would not be distinguished from its non-existence, so it would be true of it that it is existent and non-existent simultaneously.
It cannot be said: Every rational person knows by intuition the reality of these three propositions, even if this argument you mentioned does not occur to him. For we say: We do not concede that the judgment of rational people regarding these propositions is not dependent on the argument we mentioned. That is why they say: If the whole were not greater than the part, the other parts would have no effect at all; and if one thing were equal to different things, that one would be contrary to itself. This refers to what we mentioned. Indeed, they may not be able to express the argument in the way we summarized, but its meaning is established in their minds, and words are not considered.
Thus it becomes clear that the clearest of self-evident truths is our statement: Negation and affirmation do not coexist and do not cease, and it is not certain.
I say: This explanation is based on the whole being the part plus an addition, and we mean by our statement 'the whole is greater than the part' only this. So if it is an argument for the establishment of this judgment, it would be a petition of the point at issue.
I say: This is an explanation that the thing equal to different things is contrary to itself, which is exactly what he claimed to explain. If he intended proof by contradiction, then our statement 'the equal to different things is contrary to itself' is not clearer than our statement 'two things equal to a single thing are equal' so that this can be explained by that.
I say: Lack of distinction is not union. For two identicals in every aspect are not distinguishable, yet they are not one. And it would have been correct to say: If a body were in two places, then the single place would be two, and then the existence of one of the two identicals and its non-existence would be one, even though the mentioned judgment does not need this explanation.
I say: The whole is two parts, and the part is one of them, and it does not require that for something to be greater with something else than it alone, you need to know that one of the two parts has an effect first. The judgment that a thing being equal to different things necessitates its being contrary to itself is an explanation for the equality of two things to some thing being equal, but it is not more appropriate that this second explanation be an explanation for the first. For the argument should be clearer than the claim, and here there is no superiority for either of the two judgments in being clearer than the other. And the claim that everyone who conceives these propositions conceives these arguments, even if he cannot summarize them in words, is not granted.
I say: There is no doubt that it is clearer than others, and therefore the philosophers called it the first of the first, meaning in clarity. And its being clearer indicates the clarity of others, but does not indicate the need of others for it in clarity.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage provides the logical supporting arguments for three self-evident truths: (1) the whole is greater than the part, (2) things equal to the same thing are equal, (3) one body cannot be in two places at once. Each is proven by showing that its opposite leads to a contradiction (simultaneous existence and non-existence). Then an objection is raised: everyone knows these intuitively without such arguments. The response is that even intuitive knowledge depends on these arguments implicitly; people's immediate contrary-to-fact conditionals (e.g., 'if the whole were not greater...') indicate they rely on the same reasoning, just not articulated. The passage concludes that the clearest self-evident truth (non-contradiction) is not certain, a claim from the third group. The marginal notes critique each argument: the first is circular (assuming the whole is part plus addition), the second is not clearer than the original, the third confuses lack of distinction with identity. The final note concedes that non-contradiction is clearest but denies it needs the others for clarity.

Key Terms
الكل أعظم من الجزء
الأشياء المساوية
الجسم الواحد
النفي والإثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which contradiction does the proof for 'the whole is greater than the part' lead to?
A) Something being both black and not black.
B) Something being both existent and non-existent.
C) Something being in two places at once.
D) Something being equal to itself.

Key Takeaway
The three self-evident truths are not merely asserted; they are logically grounded in the principle of non-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a belief that contradicts a self-evident truth you hold, do you immediately reject it, or do you pause to examine the contradiction? Will you commit to consistently applying logical scrutiny to your own assumptions from now on?
?
If you were pressed to defend the principle 'the whole is greater than the part' to someone who denies it, would you be able to prove it from more basic principles, as Imam Razi did? How much do you rely on mere habit versus rational conviction in your daily beliefs?
?
The proofs in this passage all end in a contradiction—existence and nonexistence combined. When has your own life held a contradiction that you knew was impossible to sustain, yet you tried to live with it? Will you resolve it today by aligning your actions with the truth?

Defense of Rational Arguments
Razi answers an objection about the necessity of proofs and declares the status of the principle of non-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends the need for logical arguments even for intuitively known truths.

Core Idea
Intuitive knowledge implicitly depends on logical argument.

After presenting proofs, Razi addresses a potential objection: everyone knows these truths intuitively without formal arguments. His response is that even intuitive knowledge relies on an implicit grasp of the same reasoning; people's immediate 'if-then' statements show they use the argument subconsciously. He then concludes that the clearest self-evident truth is the principle of non-contradiction, but that principle itself is not certain (a view of the third group).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector says that every rational person knows these three truths by intuition, so the proofs are unnecessary.
Consider why someone might think proofs are superfluous.
2
Read the response
Razi argues that even intuitive knowledge is based on the same logical reasoning, but people may not articulate it clearly.
He cites the conditional statements people make as evidence.
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi states that the clearest self-evident truth is the principle of non-contradiction, but it is not certain (according to some).
This is a claim from the third group.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وأما قولنا: الأشياء المساوية للشيء الواحد متساوية، لأنه لو لم يكن كذلك لكان الألف المحكوم عليه بأنه يساوي السواد سوادا لا محالة، ومن حيث إنه محكوم عليه بأنه يساوي ما ليس بسواد يجب أن لا يكون سوادا، فلو كان الألف مساويا للأمرين لزم أن يكون الألف في نفسه سوادا وأن لا يكون في نفسه سوادا، فيجتمع النفي والإثبات.
وأما قولنا: إن الجسم الواحد في الآن الواحد لا يكون في مكانين، لأنه لو جاز ذلك لما تميز الجسم الواحد الحاصل في مكانين متبايزين عن الجسمين اللذين حصلا كذلك، وحينئذ لا يتميز وجود الجسم الآخر عن عدمه، فيصدق عليه كونه موجودا معدوما معا.

فقد لاح بأن أجل البديهيات قولنا النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان وهو غير يقيني.
أقول: هذا البيان مبني على كون الكل هو الجزء مع زيادة، ولا نعني بقولنا الكل أعظم من الجزء إلا هذا، فهو إن كان حجة على ثبوت هذا الحكم لكان مصادرة على المطلوب.
أقول: هذا بيان أن الشيء المساوي لمختلفين مخالف لنفسه، وهو عين ما ادعى بيانه، فإن أراد به البيان بالخلف فليس قولنا المساوي لمختلفين مخالف لنفسه بأوضح من قولنا المساويان لشيء بعينه متساويان حتى يتبين هذا بذاك.
أقول: عدم الامتياز لا يكون هو الاتحاد، فإن المثلين من كل جهة لا يتميزان ومع ذلك لا يكونان واحدا، وكان من الصواب أن يقول: لو كان جسم في مكانين لكان المكان الواحد اثنين، وحينئذ يكون وجود أحد المثلين وعدمه واحدا، مع أن الحكم المذكور غير محتاج إلى هذا البيان.
أقول: الكل هو جزءان، والجزء هو أحدهما، ولا يحتاج في أن الشيء مع غيره أكبر منه وحده إلى أن تعرف أن الأحد الجزأين أثر أولا، والحكم بأن كون الشيء مساويا لمختلفين مقتض لمخالفته لنفسه بيان لكون الشيئين المساويين لشيء ما متساويان ليس بأولى من أن يكون هذا الثاني بيانا للأول، فإن الحجة ينبغي أن تكون أبين من الدعوى، وليس ههنا لأحد الحكمين فضيلة في كونه أبين من الآخر، ودعوى أن كل من تصور هذه القضايا تصور هذه الحجج وإن لم يقدر على تلخيصه في العبارة غير مسلم.
أقول: لاشك في أنه أجل من غيره، ولذلك سماه الحكماء أول الأوائل، يعني في الوضوح، وكونه أوضح يدل على وضوح غيره ولا يدل على احتياج غيره في الوضوح إليه.
As for our statement: The whole is greater than the part, because if it were not so, the existence of the other part and its nonexistence would be equivalent, then that other part would simultaneously be existent and non-existent.
And as for our statement: Things equal to the same thing are equal, because if it were not so, then the Alpha which is judged to be equal to blackness is necessarily black, and from the perspective that it is judged to be equal to what is not black, it must not be black. So if the Alpha is equal to the two, it would follow that the Alpha in itself is black and not black, thus negation and affirmation coexist.
And as for our statement: One body cannot be in two places at one instant, because if that were permissible, then the single body existing in two distinct places would not be distinguished from two bodies that also exist thus, and then the existence of the other body would not be distinguished from its non-existence, so it would be true of it that it is existent and non-existent simultaneously.
It cannot be said: Every rational person knows by intuition the reality of these three propositions, even if this argument you mentioned does not occur to him. For we say: We do not concede that the judgment of rational people regarding these propositions is not dependent on the argument we mentioned. That is why they say: If the whole were not greater than the part, the other parts would have no effect at all; and if one thing were equal to different things, that one would be contrary to itself. This refers to what we mentioned. Indeed, they may not be able to express the argument in the way we summarized, but its meaning is established in their minds, and words are not considered.
Thus it becomes clear that the clearest of self-evident truths is our statement: Negation and affirmation do not coexist and do not cease, and it is not certain.
I say: This explanation is based on the whole being the part plus an addition, and we mean by our statement 'the whole is greater than the part' only this. So if it is an argument for the establishment of this judgment, it would be a petition of the point at issue.
I say: This is an explanation that the thing equal to different things is contrary to itself, which is exactly what he claimed to explain. If he intended proof by contradiction, then our statement 'the equal to different things is contrary to itself' is not clearer than our statement 'two things equal to a single thing are equal' so that this can be explained by that.
I say: Lack of distinction is not union. For two identicals in every aspect are not distinguishable, yet they are not one. And it would have been correct to say: If a body were in two places, then the single place would be two, and then the existence of one of the two identicals and its non-existence would be one, even though the mentioned judgment does not need this explanation.
I say: The whole is two parts, and the part is one of them, and it does not require that for something to be greater with something else than it alone, you need to know that one of the two parts has an effect first. The judgment that a thing being equal to different things necessitates its being contrary to itself is an explanation for the equality of two things to some thing being equal, but it is not more appropriate that this second explanation be an explanation for the first. For the argument should be clearer than the claim, and here there is no superiority for either of the two judgments in being clearer than the other. And the claim that everyone who conceives these propositions conceives these arguments, even if he cannot summarize them in words, is not granted.
I say: There is no doubt that it is clearer than others, and therefore the philosophers called it the first of the first, meaning in clarity. And its being clearer indicates the clarity of others, but does not indicate the need of others for it in clarity.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage provides the logical supporting arguments for three self-evident truths: (1) the whole is greater than the part, (2) things equal to the same thing are equal, (3) one body cannot be in two places at once. Each is proven by showing that its opposite leads to a contradiction (simultaneous existence and non-existence). Then an objection is raised: everyone knows these intuitively without such arguments. The response is that even intuitive knowledge depends on these arguments implicitly; people's immediate contrary-to-fact conditionals (e.g., 'if the whole were not greater...') indicate they rely on the same reasoning, just not articulated. The passage concludes that the clearest self-evident truth (non-contradiction) is not certain, a claim from the third group. The marginal notes critique each argument: the first is circular (assuming the whole is part plus addition), the second is not clearer than the original, the third confuses lack of distinction with identity. The final note concedes that non-contradiction is clearest but denies it needs the others for clarity.

Key Terms
البديهة
النفي والإثبات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intuitive knowledge is completely independent of logical reasoning.
Correction: Razi shows that even intuitive knowledge is rooted in logical argument, though not always articulated.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Logical argument underlies even our most basic intuitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Critical Analysis of the Proofs
Razi provides critical marginal notes on each proof and on the status of non-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will evaluate Imam Razi's marginal critiques of the three proofs and the claim about non-contradiction.

Core Idea
The proofs for self-evident truths are not immune to criticism.

In marginal notes, Razi scrutinizes the very arguments he presented. He points out that the proof for the whole being greater than the part is circular, the proof for equality is not clearer than the original claim, the proof for body in place confuses lack of distinction with identity, and other issues. The final note clarifies that although non-contradiction is the clearest, it does not imply others depend on it.

Guided Path
1
Read each critical note
There are five notes starting with 'أقول:'. Read each one and identify which original proof is being critiqued.
The first three notes critique the three proofs in order, the fourth is a broader critique, and the fifth addresses the status of non-contradiction.
2
Understand the critique of the first proof
Razi says the proof for 'whole greater than part' is circular because it assumes the definition of whole as part plus addition.
3
Understand the critique of the second proof
He argues that the proof for equality uses a claim (equal to different things is contrary to itself) that is not clearer than the original claim.
4
Understand the critique of the third proof
He notes that lack of distinction is not the same as unity; two identicals are not one. A better proof would involve place being two.
5
Reflect on the final note
Razi concedes that non-contradiction is the clearest truth, but its clarity does not mean other truths depend on it for their clarity.
This addresses the previous conclusion about the priority of non-contradiction.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قولنا: الكل أعظم من الجزء، لأنه لو لم يكن كذلك لكان وجود الجزء الآخر وعدمه بمثابة واحدة، لحينئذ يجتمع في ذلك الجزء الآخر كونه موجودا معدوما معا.
وأما قولنا: الأشياء المساوية للشيء الواحد متساوية، لأنه لو لم يكن كذلك لكان الألف المحكوم عليه بأنه يساوي السواد سوادا لا محالة، ومن حيث إنه محكوم عليه بأنه يساوي ما ليس بسواد يجب أن لا يكون سوادا، فلو كان الألف مساويا للأمرين لزم أن يكون الألف في نفسه سوادا وأن لا يكون في نفسه سوادا، فيجتمع النفي والإثبات.
وأما قولنا: إن الجسم الواحد في الآن الواحد لا يكون في مكانين، لأنه لو جاز ذلك لما تميز الجسم الواحد الحاصل في مكانين متبايزين عن الجسمين اللذين حصلا كذلك، وحينئذ لا يتميز وجود الجسم الآخر عن عدمه، فيصدق عليه كونه موجودا معدوما معا.
لا يقال: كل عاقل يعلم بالبديهة حقيقة هذه القضايا الثلاثة وإن لم يخطر بباله هذه الحجة التي ذكرتموها. لأنا نقول: لا نسلم أن حكم العقلاء بهذه القضايا غير متوقف على الحجة التي ذكرناها، ولذلك يقولون: لو لم يكن الكل أعظم من الجزء لم يكن للأجزاء الأخر أثر البتة، ولو كان الشيء الواحد مساويا لمختلفين لكان ذلك الواحد مخالفا لنفسه، وهذا إشارة إلى ما ذكرناه، نعم قد لا يمكنهم التعبير عن الحجة على الوجه الذي لخصناه، ولكن معناه مقرر في عقولهم ولا عبرة بالعبارة.
فقد لاح بأن أجل البديهيات قولنا النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان وهو غير يقيني.
أقول: هذا البيان مبني على كون الكل هو الجزء مع زيادة، ولا نعني بقولنا الكل أعظم من الجزء إلا هذا، فهو إن كان حجة على ثبوت هذا الحكم لكان مصادرة على المطلوب.
أقول: هذا بيان أن الشيء المساوي لمختلفين مخالف لنفسه، وهو عين ما ادعى بيانه، فإن أراد به البيان بالخلف فليس قولنا المساوي لمختلفين مخالف لنفسه بأوضح من قولنا المساويان لشيء بعينه متساويان حتى يتبين هذا بذاك.
أقول: عدم الامتياز لا يكون هو الاتحاد، فإن المثلين من كل جهة لا يتميزان ومع ذلك لا يكونان واحدا، وكان من الصواب أن يقول: لو كان جسم في مكانين لكان المكان الواحد اثنين، وحينئذ يكون وجود أحد المثلين وعدمه واحدا، مع أن الحكم المذكور غير محتاج إلى هذا البيان.
أقول: الكل هو جزءان، والجزء هو أحدهما، ولا يحتاج في أن الشيء مع غيره أكبر منه وحده إلى أن تعرف أن الأحد الجزأين أثر أولا، والحكم بأن كون الشيء مساويا لمختلفين مقتض لمخالفته لنفسه بيان لكون الشيئين المساويين لشيء ما متساويان ليس بأولى من أن يكون هذا الثاني بيانا للأول، فإن الحجة ينبغي أن تكون أبين من الدعوى، وليس ههنا لأحد الحكمين فضيلة في كونه أبين من الآخر، ودعوى أن كل من تصور هذه القضايا تصور هذه الحجج وإن لم يقدر على تلخيصه في العبارة غير مسلم.
أقول: لاشك في أنه أجل من غيره، ولذلك سماه الحكماء أول الأوائل، يعني في الوضوح، وكونه أوضح يدل على وضوح غيره ولا يدل على احتياج غيره في الوضوح إليه.
As for our statement: The whole is greater than the part, because if it were not so, the existence of the other part and its nonexistence would be equivalent, then that other part would simultaneously be existent and non-existent.
And as for our statement: Things equal to the same thing are equal, because if it were not so, then the Alpha which is judged to be equal to blackness is necessarily black, and from the perspective that it is judged to be equal to what is not black, it must not be black. So if the Alpha is equal to the two, it would follow that the Alpha in itself is black and not black, thus negation and affirmation coexist.
And as for our statement: One body cannot be in two places at one instant, because if that were permissible, then the single body existing in two distinct places would not be distinguished from two bodies that also exist thus, and then the existence of the other body would not be distinguished from its non-existence, so it would be true of it that it is existent and non-existent simultaneously.
It cannot be said: Every rational person knows by intuition the reality of these three propositions, even if this argument you mentioned does not occur to him. For we say: We do not concede that the judgment of rational people regarding these propositions is not dependent on the argument we mentioned. That is why they say: If the whole were not greater than the part, the other parts would have no effect at all; and if one thing were equal to different things, that one would be contrary to itself. This refers to what we mentioned. Indeed, they may not be able to express the argument in the way we summarized, but its meaning is established in their minds, and words are not considered.
Thus it becomes clear that the clearest of self-evident truths is our statement: Negation and affirmation do not coexist and do not cease, and it is not certain.
I say: This explanation is based on the whole being the part plus an addition, and we mean by our statement 'the whole is greater than the part' only this. So if it is an argument for the establishment of this judgment, it would be a petition of the point at issue.
I say: This is an explanation that the thing equal to different things is contrary to itself, which is exactly what he claimed to explain. If he intended proof by contradiction, then our statement 'the equal to different things is contrary to itself' is not clearer than our statement 'two things equal to a single thing are equal' so that this can be explained by that.
I say: Lack of distinction is not union. For two identicals in every aspect are not distinguishable, yet they are not one. And it would have been correct to say: If a body were in two places, then the single place would be two, and then the existence of one of the two identicals and its non-existence would be one, even though the mentioned judgment does not need this explanation.
I say: The whole is two parts, and the part is one of them, and it does not require that for something to be greater with something else than it alone, you need to know that one of the two parts has an effect first. The judgment that a thing being equal to different things necessitates its being contrary to itself is an explanation for the equality of two things to some thing being equal, but it is not more appropriate that this second explanation be an explanation for the first. For the argument should be clearer than the claim, and here there is no superiority for either of the two judgments in being clearer than the other. And the claim that everyone who conceives these propositions conceives these arguments, even if he cannot summarize them in words, is not granted.
I say: There is no doubt that it is clearer than others, and therefore the philosophers called it the first of the first, meaning in clarity. And its being clearer indicates the clarity of others, but does not indicate the need of others for it in clarity.
